Judgment Against an Absent Defendant (Qada' 'ala al-Gha'ib)
Explains Hanafi prohibition of ruling in absentia and Shafi'i permissibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi prohibition of judgment in absentia, explain the Hanafi proof, and compare it with the Shafi'i permissibility.

Core Idea
A judge cannot rule against an absent defendant because without denial there is no dispute, and the basis of judgment is ambiguous.

This passage from al-Hidayah (via al-Quduri) presents the Hanafi position on judgment in absentia, a key procedural rule in Islamic judiciary. While the Shafi'i school permits it based on the existence of evidence, the Hanafis insist on the presence of the defendant or a representative. The reasoning is rooted in procedural fairness: testimony aims to end a dispute, but a dispute only arises from denial, which an absent defendant cannot provide. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how each school prioritizes different aspects of justice — evidence vs. due process.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the judge shall not pass judgment against an absent defendant unless a representative is present. Note that this applies whether the defendant is absent from the city or hidden but absent from court.
The condition is the presence of a qa'im maqam (representative such as wakil or wasi).
2
Identify the opposing view
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad in some positions) permit judgment in absentia because evidence (bayyinah) exists and reveals the truth.
The Shafi'i view is based on the strength of evidence; they consider it sufficient for a ruling.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i dalil: the presence of evidence (bayyinah) makes the truth manifest, so the judge can rule. This is a straightforward evidentiary approach.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response (lana) has two parts: (1) Acting on testimony is to end a dispute, but there is no dispute without denial; since the absent defendant has not denied, no dispute exists. (2) The defendant might admit or deny, and each leads to different rulings; thus the basis is ambiguous.
The absence of denial means the case is not ripe for adjudication.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, a judgment in absentia is void and unenforceable. Under Shafi'i, it is valid and enforceable (with some conditions). The practical difference is whether the absent defendant can later challenge the ruling.
This affects how courts handle cases when defendants are unavailable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غائب پر قاضی کے فیصلہ کا حکم
قال ولا يقضى القاضى على غائب الا ان يحضر من يقوم مقامه وقال الشافعي يجوز لوجود الحجة وهى البيئة فظهر الحق ولنا ان العمل بالشهادة لقطع المنازعة ولا منازعة بدون الانكار ولم يوجد ولانه يحتمل الاقرار والانكار من الخصم فيشتبه وجه القضاء لان احكامهما مختلفة ترجمہ اور قاضی کسی غائب پر حکم نہیں کرے گا۔ مگر یہ کہ اس کا قائم مقام حاضر ہو اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ غائب پر حکم کرنا جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ حجت موجود ہے اور وہ بینہ ہے۔ پس حق ظاہر ہو گیا۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ شہادت پر عمل کرنا جھگڑا ختم کرنے کے واسطے ہوتا ہے۔ اور بغیر انکار کے جھگڑا نہیں ہوتا۔ اور انکار پایا نہیں گیا۔ اور اس لئے کہ مدعی علیہ کی طرف سے انکار اور اقرار دونوں کا احتمال ہے۔ لہذا قضاء کی وجہ سے مشتبہ ہوگی کیونکہ دونوں کے احکام مختلف ہیں۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے فرمایا کہ مدعی علیہ اگر غائب ہو خواہ شہر سے غائب ہو یا شہر میں پوشیدہ ہو مگر قاضی کی مجلس سے غائب ہو تو ہمارے نزدیک قاضی بینہ کی وجہ سے نہ اس کے خلاف حکم دے گا اور نہ موافق حکم دے گا۔ یعنی مدعی علیہ غائب کے خلاف اور موافق دونوں طرح فیصلہ کرنا نا جائز ہے۔ چنانچہ اگر قاضی نے غائب کے خلاف یا موافق کوئی فیصلہ دیا تو اس کو نافذ نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ ہاں اگر مدعی علیہ غائب کا کوئی قائم مقام موجود ہو ۔ مثلاً اس کا وکیل ہو یا وصی ہو تو اس صورت میں ہمارے نزدیک قضاء علی الغائب جائز ہے اور حضرت امام شافعی فرمایا کہ مدعی علیہ اگر شہر سے غائب ہو یا شہر میں پوشیدہ ہو مگر قاضی کی مجلس سے غائب ہو تو اس پر فیصلہ کرنا جائز ہے یہی امام مالک اور امام احمد کا قول ہے۔ اور اگر مدعی علیہ قاضی کی مجلس سے غائب ہو مگر شہر میں پوشیدہ نہ ہو بلکہ عام لوگ اس کے ٹھکانے سے واقف ہوں تو اس بارے میں حضرت امام شافعی کی دو روایتیں ہیں ایک تو یہ کہ مدعی علیہ کی حاضری کے بغیر قاضی فیصلہ نہ کرے اسی کے قائل امام مالک اور امام احمد ہیں دوسری صورت یہ کہ اس صورت میں بھی قضاء علی الغائب جائز ہے مدعی علیہ کے پوشیدہ ہونے کی
Ruling on Judgment against an Absent Party. He (al-Quduri) said: The judge shall not pass a judgment against an absent person unless someone representing him is present. Al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible, because the evidence (bayyinah) exists, so the truth has become manifest. Our proof is: Acting upon testimony is to terminate the dispute, and there is no dispute without denial, and denial has not occurred. Also, because from the defendant, both denial and admission are possible, thus the basis of the judgment becomes ambiguous, since the rulings for each are different. Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said: If the defendant is absent—whether absent from the city or hidden in the city but absent from the judge's assembly—then according to us, the judge will not rule against him or in his favor based on evidence. That is, it is not permissible to pass a judgment against or in favor of an absent defendant. Therefore, if the judge passes a ruling against or in favor of an absent party, it will not be enforced. However, if the absent defendant has a representative present, such as his agent or guardian, then in that case, judging against the absent is permissible according to us. And Imam al-Shafi'i said: If the defendant is absent from the city or hidden in the city but absent from the judge's assembly, then it is permissible to pass judgment against him. This is also the view of Imam Malik and Imam Ahmad. And if the defendant is absent from the judge's assembly but not hidden in the city, rather the general public knows his whereabouts, then concerning this, there are two narrations from Imam al-Shafi'i: one that the judge should not rule without the defendant's presence, which is also the view of Malik and Ahmad; the second is that even in this case, judging in absentia is permissible.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the permissibility of a judge passing a judgment against an absent defendant (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib). The matn (from al-Quduri) states the Hanafi position: a judge cannot rule against an absent person unless a representative (qa'im maqam) is present. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the existence of evidence (bayyinah) is sufficient to reveal the truth. The Hanafi response (lana) provides the rationale: the purpose of acting on testimony is to end a dispute, but there is no dispute without denial by the defendant. Since the absent defendant has not denied, there is no dispute. Furthermore, the defendant might admit or deny if present, and the rulings differ accordingly (admission ends the case, denial requires proof), so the basis of judgment is ambiguous. Therefore, a judgment in absentia is impermissible. The commentary by Sahib al-Quduri clarifies that this applies whether the defendant is absent from the city or hidden in the city. An exception is made if a representative (wakil or wasi) is present. Imam al-Shafi'i's view is that judgment in absentia is generally permissible, but there are two narrations when the defendant is absent from the judge's assembly but known to be in the city: one says it is not permissible (which matches Malik and Ahmad), the other says it is permissible. The passage highlights the Hanafi concern for procedural fairness and the need for a clear dispute before issuing a ruling.

Key Terms
غائب (gha'ib): An absent defendant who is not present in the judge's assembly, whether physically absent from the city or hidden within it.
قضاء علی الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): The act of a judge passing a verdict against a defendant who is not present in court.
قائم مقام (qa'im maqam): A representative of the defendant, such as a guardian (wasi) or agent (wakil), who can stand in for the absent party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition must be met for a judge to pass a judgment against an absent defendant?
A) The evidence must be strong and conclusive.
B) The defendant must be absent from the city.
C) A representative (qa'im maqam) of the defendant must be present.
D) The judge may always issue a ruling in absentia.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits judgment in absentia because the defendant's absence prevents a proper dispute from being established, ensuring procedural fairness.

Qada — Judiciary
advanced
12 min
Judgment Against an Absent Defendant (Qada' 'ala al-Gha'ib)
Inter-madhhab dispute with expansion of proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on qada' 'ala al-gha'ib, contrast it with the Shafi'i view, and evaluate the textual and rational proofs presented.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is generally impermissible in Hanafi law, except when the defendant is hidden to prevent loss of the plaintiff's right.

The question of whether a judge can issue a verdict against a defendant who is not present in court is a classic inter-madhhab dispute. In this passage, the Hanafi school defends its position — impermissible except for hidden defendants — against the Shafi'i argument that the presence of evidence alone suffices. By examining the proofs, you will understand the Hanafi method of balancing textual evidence (hadith) with legal reasoning (ma'qul).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on qada' 'ala al-gha'ib: generally impermissible (makruh tahrimi), except when the defendant is hidden and inaccessible, then permissible out of necessity.
Note the distinction between 'absent' (gha'ib) and 'hidden' (gha'ib ma' al-ikhfa').
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
The Shafi'i school permits qada' 'ala al-gha'ib absolutely. Their main proof is the hadith 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i' and the story of Hind.
Imam al-Shafi'i argues the hadith does not condition presence.
3
Follow the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof
The Hanafi response distinguishes the Hind case: the Prophet had certain knowledge of Abu Sufyan's liability, so it was a fatwa, not a general judicial precedent.
The bayyinah hadith is understood to require presence for cross-examination.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
The Hanafi proofs include: (1) Hadith of Ali requiring hearing both sides; (2) Evidence depends on defendant's ability to challenge; (3) Dispute arises from denial, absent defendant cannot deny; (4) Ambiguity between admission and denial affects judgment's finality.
Each proof addresses a different angle: textual, rational, procedural.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a Hanafi judge will not hear a case against an absent party unless the defendant is hidden; a Shafi'i judge will proceed on evidence alone.
This difference affects court procedure and protection of rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
کتاب الادب القاضي
صورت میں قضاء علی الغائب کے جائز ہونے کے درمیان اور پوشیدہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں پہلی روایت کے مطابق قضاء علی الغائب کے ناجائز ہونے کے درمیان فرق یہ ہے کہ مدعی علیہ کے پوشیدہ ہونے کی صورت میں اگر قضاء علی الغائب کو جائز نہ رکھا گیا تو مدعی کا حق ضائع ہو جائے گا۔ کیونکہ نہ مدعی علیہ حاضر ہوگا اور نہ قاضی فیصلہ کرے گا۔ اس لئے مدعی کے حقوق کو ضائع ہونے سے بچانے کے لئے مدعی علیہ کے غائب اور پوشیدہ ہونے کے باوجود قاضی کے فیصلہ کو جائز قرار دیا گیا اور جس صورت میں مدعی علیہ قاضی کی مجلس سے غائب ہو مگر شہر میں پوشیدہ نہ ہو بلکہ اس کا ٹھکانہ معلوم نہ ہو تو چونکہ اس صورت میں مدعی علیہ کو حاضر عدالت کر کے فیصلہ دینا ممکن ہے اس لئے اس صورت میں مدعی کے حقوق کے ضائع ہونے کا امکان نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب مدعی کے حقوق ضائع ہونے کا امکان نہ رہا تو قضا علی الغائب بھی جائز نہ ہوگا۔
قضاء علی الغائب کے جواز پر حضرت امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ [رسول اللہ] نے فرمایا ہے البينة على المدعى و الیمین علی من انکر یعنی قاضی کے فیصلہ کی بنیاد اور ترتیب یہ ہے اگر مدعی بیّنہ پیش کر دے تو فیصلہ اس کے حق میں ہوگا۔ اور اگر مدعی بیّنہ پیش نہ کر سکا تو مدعی علیہ سے قسم لے کر مدعی کے خلاف اور مدعی علیہ کے موافق فیصلہ کر دے۔ حدیث مذکورہ میں مدعی علیہ کی حاضری کا شرط ہونا مذکور نہیں ہے پس مدعی علیہ کی حاضری کو شرط قرار دینا حدیث رسول پر بلا دلیل زیادتی کرنا لازم آئے گا۔ حالانکہ یہ نا جائز ہے۔ پس جب مسئلہ مذکورہ میں مدعی کی جانب سے بیّنہ پیش کر دیا گیا تو حجت شرعی پائی گئی۔ اور جب حجت شرعی پائی گئی تو حق ظاہر ہو گیا اور جب حق ظاہر ہو گیا تو قاضی کو حق کے موافق یعنی مدعی کے موافق فیصلہ دینا جائز ہو گیا۔ خواہ مدعی علیہ حاضر ہو یا غائب ہو۔ امام شافعی نے حدیث ہند سے بھی استدلال کیا ہے۔ حیث قالت يا رسول الله ان ابا سفيان رجل شجيع لا يعطيني ما يكفيني وولدى فقال خذى من مال ابی سفیان مایکفیک وولدك بالمعروف یعنی ہند نے کہا اے اللہ کے رسول! ابو سفیان بڑا بخیل آدمی ہے مجھ کو اتنا بھی نفقہ نہیں دیتا جو میرے اور میرے بچہ کے لئے کافی ہو جائے۔ رسول خدا نے فرمایا ابوسفیان کے مال سے اس قدر لے لے جو تیرے اور تیرے بچہ کے لئے اوسط درجہ کی کفایت کرے۔ دیکھئے اللہ کے رسول نے ابو سفیان پر نفقہ کا حکم کیا حالانکہ ابوسفیان غائب تھا۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ قضاء علی الغائب جائز ہے؟ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ رسول اللہ ابوسفیان پر نفقہ کے استحقاق سے واقف تھے لہذا اپنی معلومات پر فتویٰ صادر فرمایا۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ محض ہندہ کی شکایت پر فیصلہ فرمایا اور ہندہ کو بیّنہ پیش کرنے کی زحمت بھی نہیں اٹھانا پڑی۔ قضاء علی الغائب کے عدم جواز پر ہماری طرف سے ابو داؤد کی وہ حدیث مستدل ہے جس میں حضرت علی کو یمن کا قاضی مقرر کرتے وقت فرمایا گیا ہے۔ لا تقض لاحد الخصمين بشئ حتى تسمع كلام الآخر فانك اذا سمعت كلام الآخر علمت كيف تقضى (رواه الترمذی و ابو داؤد) یعنی مدعی اور مدعی علیہ میں سے کسی ایک کے حق میں کوئی فیصلہ نہ دینا یہاں تک کہ دوسرے کی بات سن لے۔ پس جب تو دوسرے کی بات سن لے گا تو تجھے معلوم ہو جائے گا کہ کس طرح فیصلہ کرے۔
اس روایت سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلہ دینے کے لئے مدعی اور مدعی علیہ دونوں کی بات سننا ضروری ہے۔ اور یہ اس وقت ہوسکتا ہے جب دونوں بذات خود موجود ہوں یا قائم مقام موجود ہوں اور اگر نہ مدعی علیہ حاضر ہو اور نہ اس کا قائم مقام حاضر ہو تو اس وقت قاضی کا فیصلہ دینا درست نہ ہوگا۔
دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بیّنہ کا اس طور پر حجت ہونا کہ اس پر عمل کرنا واجب ہو اس پر موقوف ہے کہ منکر یعنی مدعی علیہ بیّنہ کو دفع کرنے اور اس میں طعن کرنے سے عاجز ہو اور مدعی علیہ کا عاجز ہونا اس وقت معلوم ہو گا جب مدعی علیہ خود موجود ہو یا اس کا نائب موجود ہو۔ پس جب مدعی علیہ اور اس کا نائب دونوں غائب ہوں تو اس کا عجز معلوم نہیں ہو گا۔ اور جب اس کا عجز معلوم نہیں ہوا تو بیّنہ کا حجت شرعی ہونا بھی ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب مدعی علیہ اور نائب کی عدم موجودگی میں بیّنہ کا حجت شرعی ہونا ظاہر نہیں ہوا تو اس بیّنہ کی وجہ سے مدعی کے حق میں قاضی کا فیصلہ بھی درست نہ ہوگا۔ اس دلیل سے معلوم ہوا کہ قضاء علی الغائب نا جائز ہے۔ (فتح القدير) تیسری دلیل جس کو صاحب ہدایہ نے ذکر کیا ہے یہ ہے کہ شہادت اور بیّنہ پر عمل فقط جھگڑا ختم کرنے کے واسطے جائز ہے اور جھگڑا اس وقت پیدا ہو گا جب مدعی علیہ، مدعی کے دعویٰ کا انکار کرے گا۔ اور مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں انکار پایا نہیں گیا پس جب مدعی علیہ کے غائب ہونے کی وجہ سے انکار نہیں پایا گیا اور انکار نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے جھگڑا پیدا نہیں ہوا تو شہادت پر عمل کرنا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا یعنی مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں اگر قاضی نے فیصلہ دیدیا تو وہ نافذ نہ ہوگا اور اس پر عمل کرنا جائز نہ ہوگا۔ اس دلیل سے بھی ثابت ہوا کہ قضاء علی الغائب جائز نہیں ہے۔ یہاں یہ اشکال ہو سکتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ اگر حاضر ہو اور مدعی کے دعویٰ پر سکوت کرے اور قاضی مدعی کے بینہ پر فیصلہ کر دے تو یہ فیصلہ واجب العمل اور قابل تنفیذ ہوتا ہے۔ پس بغیر انکار اور جھگڑے کے شہادت پر عمل کرنا پایا گیا۔ حالانکہ آپ نے فرمایا ہے کہ بغیر جھگڑے کے شہادت پر عمل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اور جھگڑا بغیر انکار کے نہیں ہوتا؟ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ یہاں مدعی علیہ کا سکوت، انکار کے مرتبہ میں ہے کیونکہ مسلمان کا ظاہر حال یہ ہے کہ اگر اس پر کسی کا کوئی حق ہو تو سکوت نہ کرے بلکہ اس کا اقرار کر لے۔ پس مدعی علیہ کا سکوت کرنا اپنے اوپر وجوب حق کا انکار کرتا ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ اس موقع پر مدعی علیہ کا سکوت اس کے انکار کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔
چوتھی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مدعی علیہ جو غائب ہے اس کی طرف سے بھی یہ احتمال ہے کہ وہ مدعی کے حق اور دعویٰ کا اقرار کرے اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ انکار کرے پس چونکہ اقرار اور انکار کی وجہ سے قاضی کا فیصلہ مختلف ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے قضاء کی جہت مشتبہ ہو جائے گی۔ یعنی مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں یہ کہا جائے گا کہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ، مدعی کے دعویٰ کا اقرار کرے۔ اور قاضی اقرار کی وجہ سے فیصلہ دے۔ حالانکہ بیّنہ کی وجہ سے جو فیصلہ دیا جاتا ہے وہ خود مدعی علیہ پر اور تمام لوگوں پر واقع ہوتا ہے۔ اور اقرار کی وجہ سے جو فیصلہ دیا جاتا ہے وہ فقط مدعی علیہ پر واقع ہوتا ہے اس کے علاوہ پر واقع نہیں ہوتا۔ قضاء مع البینہ اور قضاء بالا قرار کے درمیان یہ بھی فرق ہے کہ اگر قاضی نے مدعی علیہ کے اقرار پر فیصلہ دیا تو آئندہ مدعی علیہ کو اپنی برات کے گواہ قائم کرنے کا حق نہ ہو گا۔ اور اگر قاضی نے مدعی علیہ کے انکار کی وجہ سے بیّنہ پر فیصلہ دیا تو آئندہ مدعی علیہ اپنی برات کے گواہ قائم کر سکتا ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی نے باندی خریدی۔ اور اس باندی نے مشتری کے پاس بچہ جنا پھر ایک آدمی اس باندی کا مستحق ہو گیا پس اگر مستحق کا استحقاق بیّنہ کے ذریعہ ثابت ہوا تو مستحق اس باندی کو بھی لے گا اور اس کے بچہ کو بھی لے گا۔ اور اگر مشتری نے مستحق کے استحقاق کا اقرار کیا ہو تو مستحق فقط باندی کو لینے کا مجاز ہو گا۔ اور اس کے بچہ کو لینے کا مجاز نہ ہو گا۔
Book of the Etiquette of the Judge. In the case of the permissibility of judgment against the absent [defendant] and in the case of his not being hidden, the difference according to the first narration regarding the impermissibility of judgment against the absent is that when the defendant is hidden, if judgment against the absent is not permitted, the plaintiff's right would be lost. Because neither the defendant will be present nor will the judge decide. Therefore, to protect the plaintiff's rights from being lost, the judge's decision was declared permissible despite the defendant's absence and hiddenness. And in the case where the defendant is absent from the judge's assembly but is not hidden in the city, rather his whereabouts are unknown, then since in that case it is possible to bring the defendant to court and give a verdict, in that case there will be no possibility of the plaintiff's rights being lost. And when there is no possibility of the plaintiff's rights being lost, then judgment against the absent will also not be permissible. Regarding the permissibility of judgment against the absent, Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that the Messenger of Allah said: 'The burden of proof is on the plaintiff, and the oath is on the one who denies.' That is, the basis and sequence of the judge's decision is that if the plaintiff presents evidence, the decision will be in his favor. And if the plaintiff cannot present evidence, then the judge takes an oath from the defendant and decides against the plaintiff and in favor of the defendant. In the mentioned hadith, the condition of the defendant's presence is not mentioned. So making the defendant's presence a condition would necessitate an addition to the hadith of the Messenger without proof, while this is impermissible. Thus, in the mentioned issue, since the plaintiff presented evidence, a legal proof was found. And when a legal proof was found, the right became manifest. And when the right became manifest, it became permissible for the judge to decide in accordance with the right, i.e., in favor of the plaintiff, whether the defendant is present or absent. Imam al-Shafi'i also argued from the hadith of Hind: when she said, 'O Messenger of Allah, Abu Sufyan is a very stingy man; he does not give me enough for me and my child.' He said, 'Take from the wealth of Abu Sufyan what is sufficient for you and your child in a reasonable manner.' That is, Hind said, 'O Messenger of Allah! Abu Sufyan is a very miserly man; he does not give me enough maintenance for me and my child.' The Messenger of God said, 'Take from Abu Sufyan's wealth as much as suffices you and your child in an average manner.' Observe that the Messenger of God gave a ruling for maintenance against Abu Sufyan while Abu Sufyan was absent. Thus it is known that judgment against the absent is permissible? The answer to this is that the Messenger of Allah was aware of Abu Sufyan's liability for maintenance, so he issued a fatwa based on his knowledge. This is why he decided based solely on Hind's complaint, and Hind did not have to bear the trouble of presenting evidence. As for the impermissibility of judgment against the absent, our proof is the hadith of Abu Dawud in which when appointing Ali as judge of Yemen, he said: 'Do not give judgment for either of the two litigants until you hear the statement of the other, for when you hear the statement of the other, you will know how to judge.' (Narrated by Tirmidhi and Abu Dawud) That is, do not give any judgment in favor of either the plaintiff or the defendant until you hear the other's statement. Then when you hear the other's statement, you will know how to decide. From this narration it is known that for giving a verdict, it is necessary to hear the statements of both the plaintiff and the defendant. And this can only happen when both are present in person or their representatives are present. And if neither the defendant is present nor his representative, then the judge's decision will not be valid. The second proof is that the evidence being a proof such that acting upon it is obligatory depends on the fact that the denier, i.e., the defendant, is unable to refute the evidence and challenge it. And the defendant's inability will be known when the defendant himself is present or his deputy is present. So when both the defendant and his deputy are absent, his inability will not be known. And when his inability is not known, then the evidence being a valid legal proof will not be established. And when in the absence of the defendant and his deputy, the evidence's being a legal proof is not apparent, then the judge's decision in favor of the plaintiff based on that evidence will also not be valid. From this proof it is known that judgment against the absent is invalid. (Fath al-Qadir) The third proof mentioned by the author of al-Hidayah is that acting upon testimony and evidence is only permissible for ending a dispute, and a dispute arises only when the defendant denies the plaintiff's claim. And in the absence of the defendant, denial is not found. So when due to the defendant's absence, denial is not found, and due to the absence of denial, no dispute arises, then acting upon testimony will not be permissible. That is, if the judge gives a decision in the absence of the defendant, it will not be enforceable and acting upon it will not be permissible. This proof also establishes that judgment against the absent is not permissible. Here an objection can be raised: if the defendant is present and remains silent on the plaintiff's claim, and the judge decides based on the plaintiff's evidence, then this decision is binding and enforceable. Thus we see acting upon testimony occurring without denial and dispute, although you said that acting upon testimony is not permissible without a dispute, and a dispute does not occur without denial. The answer to this is that the defendant's silence is equivalent to denial because the apparent state of a Muslim is that if someone has a right over him, he does not remain silent but rather acknowledges it. So the defendant's silence constitutes denial of the obligation of the right upon him. Thus it is known that in this situation, the defendant's silence is equivalent to his denial. The fourth proof is that regarding the absent defendant, there is the possibility that he may admit the plaintiff's right and claim, and also the possibility that he may deny. Since the judge's decision differs based on admission and denial, the direction of judgment will become ambiguous. That is, in the absence of the defendant, it could be said that perhaps the defendant admits the plaintiff's claim, and the judge decides based on admission, whereas a decision based on evidence applies both to the defendant and to all people. But a decision based on admission applies only to the defendant, not to others. There is also this difference between judgment with evidence and judgment with admission: if the judge gave a decision based on the defendant's admission, then afterwards the defendant has no right to establish witnesses for his innocence. And if the judge gave a decision based on evidence due to the defendant's denial, then afterwards the defendant can establish witnesses for his innocence. Similarly, if someone bought a slave-girl, and she gave birth to a child with the buyer, then a person becomes entitled to the slave-girl. So if the claimant's entitlement is established through evidence, the claimant will take both the slave-girl and her child. And if the buyer admitted the claimant's entitlement, then the claimant is only entitled to take the slave-girl, not her child.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute on the permissibility of qada' 'ala al-gha'ib, i.e., a judge issuing a verdict against an absent defendant. The Hanafi position, as elaborated, generally holds that such judgment is not permissible, except when the defendant is hidden and inaccessible, in which case it is allowed to protect the plaintiff's right. The passage opens by distinguishing between two scenarios: when the defendant is hidden (gha'ib ma' al-ikhfa') and when he is merely absent but not hidden. In the first, judgment is permissible to prevent loss of the plaintiff's right; in the second, it is not, because the defendant can be brought to court. The author then presents the Shafi'i argument based on the famous hadith 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' (the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies). The Shafi'i view is that since the hadith does not condition the defendant's presence, judgment based on evidence is valid whether the defendant is present or absent. They also cite the story of Hind and Abu Sufyan, where the Prophet ordered Hind to take maintenance from Abu Sufyan's wealth despite his absence. The Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof is nuanced: the Hind case is specific because the Prophet had knowledge of Abu Sufyan's obligation; it was not a general judicial ruling but a fatwa based on certain knowledge. Thus it cannot be used as a general precedent. The Hanafi proofs start with the hadith of Ali, narrated by Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi, where the Prophet instructed Ali not to judge for one party without hearing the other. This implies that both parties must be present or have their representatives present. The second Hanafi proof is legal reasoning: the validity of evidence (bayyinah) as a hujjah shar'iyyah requires that the defendant be present to challenge it; otherwise, the truth remains uncertain. The third proof is that the purpose of testimony is to resolve a dispute, and a dispute only arises from denial; an absent defendant cannot deny, so there is no dispute to resolve. An objection is raised: what if the defendant is present but silent? The answer is that silence is equivalent to denial because a Muslim's normal conduct is to admit a right against him; thus silence implies denial. The fourth proof is that an absent defendant might either admit or deny, leading to two different types of judgment (based on iqrar or bayyinah), which have different effects on finality and possibility of appeal. This ambiguity renders the judgment unsound. The passage also cites Fath al-Qadir as a source for part of the argument. Overall, the Hanafi school maintains that qada' 'ala al-gha'ib is impermissible except when the defendant is hidden and inaccessible, based on these proofs.

Key Terms
قضاء علی الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): Judgment given by a judge against a defendant who is not present in court at the time of hearing.
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence presented to prove a claim in court, typically testimony of witnesses.
مدعی علیہ (mudda'a 'alayhi): The defendant against whom a claim is made in court.
انکار (inkar): The act of denying the plaintiff's claim by the defendant.
سکوت (sukut): Remaining silent when faced with a claim; in legal context, silence is interpreted as denial if the claimant has a valid right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, when is judgment against an absent defendant (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib) permissible?
A) It is never permissible under any circumstances.
B) It is always permissible if the plaintiff presents evidence.
C) It is permissible only when the defendant is hidden and inaccessible.
D) It is permissible only if the defendant has a representative present.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, judgment against an absent defendant is generally impermissible unless the defendant is hidden, balancing the need to protect the plaintiff's right with the defendant's right to be heard.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
12 min
Impermissibility of Judging in Absentia (Qada' 'ala al-Gha'ib)
Establishes prohibition of qada' 'ala al-gha'ib with proofs and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judging in absentia, explain the rational proofs, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the condition of denial at the time of judgment.

Core Idea
A judge cannot pass a valid verdict against an absent defendant; the condition of a present dispute is essential.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah establishes the prohibition of qada' 'ala al-gha'ib. The author provides several rational proofs to show that the presence of the defendant (or his representative) is a fundamental condition for a valid judgment. Even when the defendant initially denies the claim and then disappears, the majority Hanafi position holds that the judge cannot proceed, while Imam Abu Yusuf argues otherwise. Your goal is to understand the reasoning behind each proof and the resolution of the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall ruling
The passage explicitly states that judging in absentia is impermissible. State the ruling: the verdict is invalid (batil) and unenforceable.
2
Analyze the first two proofs
First proof: the judge must hear both parties, which is impossible if the defendant is absent. Second proof: evidence (bayyinah) only becomes binding when the defendant is present to challenge it; his incapacity is unknown in absence.
3
Analyze the third proof and its objection
Third proof: testimony is only to resolve a dispute, which requires denial by the defendant. Objection: defendant's silence is considered denial if he is present. Resolution: in absence, silence cannot be confirmed as denial.
4
Analyze the fourth proof
The absent defendant might confess or deny, creating ambiguity in the judge's course. The effects of a verdict differ between confession and denial, so presence is required to remove ambiguity.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
If the defendant denies and then disappears before judgment, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say the condition of denial at the time of judgment is not met. Abu Yusuf says istishab presumes continuation of denial. The commentators refute Abu Yusuf: istishab cannot establish a new condition.
6
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: even after initial denial and disappearance, the judge cannot give a verdict in absentia. This is the practical ruling for fatwa.

كتاب الادب القاضي
اشرف الہدایہ جلد-نم
اس روایت سے معلوم ہوا کہ فیصلہ دینے کے لئے مدعی اور مدعی علیہ دونوں کی بات سننا ضروری ہے۔ اور یہ اس وقت ہوسکتا ہے جب دونوں بذات خود موجود ہوں یا قائم مقام موجود ہوں اور اگر نہ مدعی علیہ حاضر ہو اور نہ اس کا قائم مقام حاضر ہو تو اس وقت قاضی کا فیصلہ دینا درست نہ ہوگا۔
دوسری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بینہ کا اس طور پر حجت ہونا کہ اس پر عمل کرنا واجب ہو اس پر موقوف ہے کہ منکر یعنی مدعی علیہ بینہ و دفع کرنے اور اس میں طعن کرنے سے عاجز ہو اور مدعی علیہ کا عاجز ہونا اس وقت معلوم ہو گا جب مدعی علیہ خود موجود ہو یا اس کا نائب موجود ہو ۔ پس جب مدعی علیہ اور اس کا نائب دونوں غائب ہوں تو اس کا عجز معلوم نہیں ہو گا ۔ اور جب اس کا عجز معلوم نہیں ہوا تو بینہ کا حجت شرعی ہونا بھی ثابت نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب مدعلی علیہ اور نائب کی عدم موجودگی میں بینہ کا حجت شرعی ہونا ظاہر نہیں ہوا تو اس ہینہ کی وجہ سے مدعی کے حق میں قاضی کا فیصلہ بھی درست نہ ہوگا۔ اس دلیل سے معلوم ہوا کہ قضاء علی الغائب نا جائز ہے۔ (فتح القدير ) تیسری دلیل جس کو صاحب ہدایہ نے ذکر کیا ہے یہ ہے کہ شہادت اور ہینہ پر عمل فقط جھگڑا ختم کرنے کے واسطے جائز ہے اور جھگڑا اس وقت پیدا ہو گا جب مدعی علیہ، مدعی کے دعوئی کا انکار کرے گا۔ اور مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں انکار پایا نہیں گیا پس جب مدلی علیہ کے غائب ہونے کی وجہ سے انکار نہیں پایا گیا اور انکار نہ پائے جانے کی وجہ سے جھگڑا پیدا نہیں ہوا تو شہادت پر عمل کرنا بھی جائز نہ ہوگا یعنی مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں اگر قاضی نے فیصلہ دیدیا تو وہ نافذ نہ ہوگا اور اس پر عمل کرنا جائز نہ ہوگا۔ اس دلیل سے بھی ثابت ہوا کہ قضاء علی الغائب جائز نہیں ہے۔ یہاں یہ اشکال ہو سکتا ہے کہ مدعی علیہ اگر حاضر ہو اور مدعی کے دعوئی پر سکوت کرے اور قاضی مدعی کے بینہ پر فیصلہ کر دے تو یہ فیصلہ واجب العمل اور قابل تنفیذ ہوتا ہے۔ پس بغیر انکار اور جھگڑے کے شہادت پر عمل کرنا پایا گیا۔ حالانکہ آپ نے فرمایا ہے کہ بغیر جھگڑے کے شہادت پر عمل کرنا جائز نہیں ہے۔ اور جھگڑا بغیر انکار کے نہیں ہوتا ؟ اس کا جواب یہ ہے کی یہاں مدعلی علیہ کا سکوت، انکار کے مرتبہ میں ہے کیونکہ مسلمان کا ظاہر حال یہ ہے کہ اگر اس پر کسی کا کوئی حق ہو تو سکوت نہ کرے بلکہ اس کا اقرار کر لے۔ پس مدعی علیہ کا سکوت کرنا اپنے اوپر وجوب حق کا انکار کرتا ہے۔ پس معلوم ہوا کہ اس موقع پر مدعی علیہ کا سکوت اس کے انکار کے مرتبہ میں ہے۔
چوتھی دلیل یہ ہے کہ مدعی علیہ جو غائب ہے اس کی طرف سے بھی یہ احتمال ہے کہ وہ مدعی کے حق اور دعوئی کا اقرار کرے اور یہ بھی احتمال ہے کہ انکار کرے پس چونکہ اقرار اور انکار کی وجہ سے قاضی کا فیصلہ مختلف ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے قضاء کی جہت مشتبہ ہو جائے گی۔ یعنی مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں یہ کہا جائے گا کر بھی ہو سکتا ہے کہ بدعی علیہ، مدعی کے دعوئی کا اقرار کرے۔ اور قاضی اقرار کی وجہ سے فیصلہ دے۔ حالانکہ بینہ کی وجہ سے جو فیصلہ دیا جاتا ہے وہ خود مدعی علیہ پر اور تمام لوگوں پر واقع ہوتا ہے۔ اور اقرار کی وجہ سے جو فیصلہ دیا جاتا ہے وہ فقط مدعی علیہ پر واقع ہوتا ہے اس کے علاوہ پر واقع نہیں ہوتا۔ قضاء مع البینہ اور قضاء بالا قرار کے درمیان یہ بھی فرق ہے کہ اگر قاضی نے مدعی علیہ کے اقرار پر فیصلہ دیا تو آئندہ مدعی علیہ کو اپنی برات کے گواہ قائم کرنے کا حق نہ ہو گا۔ اور اگر قاضی نے مدعی علیہ کے انکار کی وجہ سے ہینہ پر فیصلہ دیا تو آئندہ مدعی علیہ اپنی برات کے گواہ قائم کر سکتا ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر کسی نے باندی خریدی۔ اور اس باندی نے مشتری کے پاس بچہ جنا پھر ایک آدمی اس باندی کا مستحق ہو گیا پس اگر مستحق کا استحقاق بینہ کے ذریعہ ثابت ہوا تو مستم اس باندی کو بھی لے گا اور اس کے بچہ کو بھی لے گا۔ اور اگر مشتری نے مستحق کے استحقاق کا اقرار کیا ہو تو مستحق فقط باندی کو لینے کا مجاز ہو گا۔ اور اُس کے بچہ کو لینے کا مجاز نہ ہوگا۔ بہر حال خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ اقرار مدعی علیہ اور انکار مدعی علیہ کی وجہ سے احکام قضا مختلف ہوتے ہیں۔ پس مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں قضائے قاضی کی جہت چونکہ مشتبہ ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے مدعی علیہ کا حاضر ہونا ضروری ہے ۔ اور جب مدعی علیہ کا حاضر ہونا ضروری ہے تو قضاء علی الغائب نا جائز ہے۔
مدعی علیہ نے دعوئی کا انکار کیا اور قاضی کے فیصلہ سے پہلے غائب ہو گیا تو بھی یہی فیصلہ ہے ولو انكر ثم غاب فكذلك الجواب لان الشرط قيام الانكار وقت القضاء و فيه خلاف ابي يوسف ومن يقوم مقامه قد يكون نائباً بانابته كالوكيل او بانابة الشرع كالوصى من جهة القاضي وقد يكون حكما بان كان ما يدعى على الغائب سبباً لما يدعيه على الحاضر وهذا في غير صورة في الكتب اما اذا كان شرطاً لحقه فلا يعتبر به في جعله خصماً عن الغائب و قد عرف تمامه في الجامع
ترجمہ اگر مدعلی علیہ نے انکار کیا اور پھر غائب ہو گیا تو بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ اس لئے کہ قضاء کے وقت انکار موجود ہونا شرط ہے اور اس میں امام ابو یوسف کا اختلاف ہے اور جو شخص مدعی علیہ کا قائم مقام ہو وہ کبھی اس کے نائب کرنے سے نائب ہوتا ہے جیسے وکیل یا شریعت کے نائب کرنے سے جیسے وصی ، قاضی کی طرف اور بھی حکما نا ئب ہوتا ہے۔ بایں طور کہ جس چیز کا غائب پر دعویٰ اور وہ اس چیز کا سبب ہو ، جس کا حاضر پر دعوی کرتا ہے اور یہ کتابوں میں بہت سی صورتوں میں مذکور ہے۔ بہر حال اگر مدعی کے ثبوت حق کی شرط ہو تو غائب کی طرف سے حاضر کو نائب بنانے میں اس شرط کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا اور یہ تمام کلام جامع صغیر میں ہے۔ تشریح صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر مدعلی علیہ نے دعوی کا انکار کیا اور قاضی کے فیصلہ دینے سے پہلے غائب ہو گیا تو بھی یہی حکم ہے کہ قاضی فیصلہ نہ دے ورنہ اس کا فیصلہ نافذ نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ قضاء کے وقت انکار کا موجود ہونا شرط ہے حالانکہ مدعی علیہ کے غائب ہونے کی وجہ سے قضاء کے وقت، مدعی علیہ کی طرف سے انکار نہیں پایا گیا۔ پس جب قضاء کی شرط نہیں پائی گئی تو اس صورت میں قضائے قاضی بھی جائز نہ ہوگا۔ لیکن اس صورت میں حضرت امام ابو یوسف کا اختلاف ہے یعنی ان کے نزدیک مذکورہ صورت میں قاضی فیصلہ دے سکتا ہے۔ کیونکہ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ قضائے قاضی کے لئے وقت قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار شرط ہے۔ اور مدعی علیہ انکار کرنے کے بعد جب غائب ہو گیا اور وقت قضاء تک حاضر نہیں ہوا تو استصحاب کی وجہ سے یعنی سابق حالت پر باقی رکھنے کی وجہ سے یہی کہا جائے گا کہ وہ اپنے انکار پر قائم اور مصر ہے۔ پس جب قاضی کے فیصلہ دینے کی شرط یعنی وقت قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار پایا گیا تو قاضی کا فیصلہ دینا بھی جائز ہوگا۔
امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل کا جواب دیتے ہوئے صاحب عنایہ اور علامہ بدرالدین عینی صاحب بنایہ نے فرمایا کی دلیل استصحاب کے ذریعہ کسی چیز کو رفع تو کیا جا سکتا ہے مگر ثابت نہیں کیا جا سکتا۔ اور یہاں حکم قضاء کو ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت ہے نہ کہ رفع کرنے کی ۔ پس اس جگہ استصحاب کے ذریعہ وقتِ قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار ثابت نہ ہو گا ۔ اور جب وقت قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار ثابت نہیں ہوا تو قضائے قاضی کی شرط بھی نہیں پائی گئی اور جب قضائے قاضی کی شرط نہیں پائی گئی تو مذکورہ صورت میں قاضی کا فیصلہ دینا بھی نا جائز ہوگا۔
It is learned from this narration that to give a verdict, it is necessary to hear both the plaintiff and the defendant. And this is possible only when both are present in person or their representatives are present. And if neither the defendant nor his representative is present, then the judge's verdict will not be valid.
The second proof is that evidence (bayyinah) being a proof (hujjah) in such a way that acting upon it is obligatory depends upon the fact that the denier (i.e., the defendant) is incapable of refuting the evidence and objecting to it. And the defendant's incapability will be known only when the defendant himself is present or his deputy is present. So when both the defendant and his deputy are absent, his incapability will not be known. And when his incapability is not known, then the evidence being a legal proof (hujjah shar'iyyah) will not be established. And when the evidence being a legal proof is not apparent in the absence of the defendant and his deputy, then the judge's verdict in favor of the plaintiff based on that evidence will also not be valid. From this proof it is learned that judging in absentia (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib) is impermissible. (Fath al-Qadir)
The third proof that the author of al-Hidayah has mentioned is that acting upon testimony (shahadah) and evidence is only permissible to end a dispute, and a dispute arises only when the defendant denies the plaintiff's claim. And in the absence of the defendant, denial is not found. So when denial is not found due to the defendant's absence, and a dispute does not arise due to the absence of denial, then acting upon testimony will also be impermissible. That is, if the judge gives a verdict in the defendant's absence, it will not be enforceable and acting upon it will not be permissible. From this proof also it is established that judging in absentia is not permissible.
Here an objection may arise: If the defendant is present and remains silent upon the plaintiff's claim, and the judge gives a verdict based on the plaintiff's evidence, this verdict is obligatory to act upon and enforceable. So acting upon testimony has been found without denial and dispute, whereas you have said that acting upon testimony without dispute is not permissible, and dispute does not occur without denial? The answer to this is that here the defendant's silence is at the level of denial, because the normal state of a Muslim is that if someone has a right upon him, he would not remain silent but would confess. So the defendant's silence constitutes denial of the obligation of the right upon him. So it is learned that in this situation the defendant's silence is at the level of his denial.
The fourth proof is that regarding the absent defendant, there is a probability that he may confess to the plaintiff's right and claim, and also a probability that he may deny. Since the judge's verdict differs due to confession and denial, the direction of judgment becomes ambiguous. That is, in the absence of the defendant, it could be said that perhaps the defendant would confess to the plaintiff's claim, and the judge would give a verdict based on confession, whereas a verdict given based on evidence is effective upon the defendant and all people, while a verdict given based on confession is effective only upon the defendant and not upon others. Another difference between judgment with evidence and judgment with confession is that if the judge gave a verdict based on the defendant's confession, then later the defendant does not have the right to produce witnesses for his innocence. But if the judge gave a verdict based on evidence due to the defendant's denial, then later the defendant can produce witnesses for his innocence. Similarly, if someone bought a slave-girl and she gave birth to a child at the buyer's place, then a man became entitled to that slave-girl. So if the entitlement of the claimant is established by evidence, the claimant will take both the slave-girl and her child. But if the buyer confessed to the claimant's entitlement, the claimant will be entitled only to take the slave-girl, and will not be entitled to take her child. In any case, the summary is that due to the defendant's confession and denial, the rulings of judgment differ. Therefore, in the absence of the defendant, since the direction of the judge's judgment becomes ambiguous, the defendant's presence is necessary. And when the defendant's presence is necessary, judging in absentia is impermissible.
If the defendant denied the claim and then disappeared before the judge's verdict, the ruling is the same: "And if he denied then disappeared, the answer is likewise, because the condition is the existence of denial at the time of judgment, and there is a difference of opinion from Abu Yusuf." And the one who stands in his place (qaim maqam) may be a deputy by his appointment, like an agent (wakil), or by appointment of the law, like an executor (wasi) from the judge's side, and may be a deputy by ruling (hukman) in that the thing claimed against the absent person is a cause for what he claims against the present person. And this is mentioned in many forms in the books. However, if it is a condition for the plaintiff's proof of right, then this condition is not considered in making the present person a deputy on behalf of the absent one. And this entire discussion is in al-Jami' al-Saghir.
Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah said that if the defendant denied the claim and then disappeared before the judge gave the verdict, the ruling is the same: the judge should not give a verdict; otherwise his verdict will not be enforceable. Because the existence of denial at the time of judgment is a condition, whereas due to the defendant's absence, denial from the defendant's side is not found at the time of judgment. So when the condition of judgment is not fulfilled, the judge's verdict will also not be permissible in this case. However, in this case, Imam Abu Yusuf has a difference of opinion, meaning that according to him, in the mentioned case the judge can give a verdict. Because Imam Abu Yusuf says that for the judge's judgment, persistence on denial until the time of judgment is a condition. And when the defendant disappeared after denying and did not appear until the time of judgment, then due to istishab (presumption of continuity), i.e., by keeping him on the previous state, it will be said that he remains firm and insistent on his denial. So when the condition for the judge's verdict, i.e., persistence on denial until the time of judgment, is found, then the judge's verdict is permissible.
Responding to Imam Abu Yusuf's proof, the authors of al-'Inayah and Badr al-Din al-'Ayni, the author of al-Binayah, said that through istishab, something can be removed but cannot be established. And here the need is to establish the ruling of judgment, not to remove it. Therefore, in this place, persistence on denial until the time of judgment is not established by istishab. And when persistence on denial until the time of judgment is not established, the condition for the judge's judgment is also not fulfilled. And when the condition for the judge's judgment is not fulfilled, then in the mentioned case, the judge's verdict is also impermissible.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah, commentary on al-Hidayah, discusses the ruling on judging in absentia (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib). The author establishes that it is impermissible for a judge to pass a verdict against an absent defendant. He provides four rational proofs. First, a judge must hear both parties; their presence or that of their representatives is essential. Without the defendant, the judge cannot fulfill this requirement. Second, evidence (bayyinah) only becomes a binding proof (hujjah) when the defendant is present to have the opportunity to refute it; his incapacity to do so is only known if he is present. Third, the purpose of testimony is to resolve a dispute, which only arises when the defendant denies the claim; without the defendant, denial is absent, so testimony has no basis. The author addresses an objection: if the defendant is present but silent, his silence is considered a denial because a Muslim would normally confess if the claim were true. Fourth, since the absent defendant might confess or deny, and the effects of a verdict differ between confession and denial, the judge's proper course is ambiguous. Specific examples are given: if a verdict is based on confession, the defendant cannot later claim innocence, whereas if based on evidence (when he denied), he can. Also, if a slave-girl is claimed, the ruling on her child differs based on whether entitlement is proven by evidence or confession.

The author then addresses a specific sub-case: the defendant denies the claim and then disappears before the judge issues a verdict. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the ruling remains the same — the judge cannot give a verdict — because the condition of denial existing at the time of judgment is not met. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, arguing that the defendant's prior denial persists through istishab (presumption of continuity). However, the commentators ('Inayah, Binayah) refute this, stating that istishab can only maintain a pre-existing state, not establish a new ruling or condition. Since the ruling of a valid verdict requires the condition of denial at the moment of judgment, and istishab cannot prove that condition is met, the verdict remains invalid. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that even in this case, judging in absentia is not permissible.

The passage also clarifies that a representative (qaim maqam) of the defendant can be either appointed (like a wakil) or legal (like a wasi), and in some cases a present person may be treated as a deputy of an absent defendant if the claim against the absent person is a cause for the claim against the present one. However, this does not apply if the condition for proof of right is involved — then the present person is not considered a deputy.

Key Terms
قضاء علی الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): The act of a judge passing a verdict against a defendant who is not present in court, either in person or through a representative.
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, usually the testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, that establishes a claim in court.
استصحاب (istishab): Presumption of continuity; a legal maxim that a state of affairs existing in the past is presumed to continue until the contrary is proven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling if a defendant denies a claim and then disappears before the judge issues a verdict?
A) The judge can proceed and give a verdict based on the initial denial continuing through istishab.
B) The judge cannot give a valid verdict because the denial must be present at the time of judgment.
C) The judge should postpone the case until the defendant returns but can issue a verdict if evidence is strong.
D) The judge can give a verdict but must allow the defendant to produce witnesses later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits judging in absentia because the condition of a present dispute is essential, and even the initial denial cannot be presumed to continue at the time of judgment.

Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judgment against an absent defendant, identify the condition required for valid judgment, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the use of istishab.

Core Idea
Judgment against an absent defendant is invalid; istishab cannot establish the required condition of denial at the time of judgment.

This lesson addresses a key procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh: the prohibition of passing judgment against an absent defendant. The passage explains the underlying condition—the defendant's presence to deny the claim—and then explores a nuanced disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf about whether presumption of continuity (istishab) can substitute for actual presence. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how conditions (shurut) operate in legal rulings and the limits of rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
State the General Ruling
Identify the main ruling: judgment against an absent defendant is impermissible (ghayr ja'iz). The text says 'qada' 'ala al-gha'ib la ja'iz'.
This is the basic rule; exceptions exist with representatives.
2
Identify the Condition
The condition for valid judgment is the existence of denial (inkar) at the time of judgment. The defendant must be present to deny the claim.
Admission (iqrar) leads to a different ruling.
3
Apply to the Specific Case
If the defendant denies the claim and then disappears before the judge decides, the same rule applies. The condition is not met because denial at the time of judgment is absent.
This is the scenario that triggers the dispute.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees: he considers persistence on denial (istiqrar 'ala al-inkar) until the time of judgment as the condition. He uses istishab (presumption of continuity) to establish that persistence.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold the majority view.
5
Analyze the Refutation
Later scholars (al-'Inayah, al-Binayah) refute Abu Yusuf: istishab can only remove a legal state, not establish one. Here, we need to establish persistence (a new legal fact), which istishab cannot do.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows Abu Hanifa.

بہر حال خلاصہ یہ ہے کہ اقرار مدعی علیہ اور انکار مدعی علیہ کی وجہ سے احکام قضا مختلف ہوتے ہیں۔ پس مدعی علیہ کی عدم موجودگی میں قضائے قاضی کی جہت چونکہ مشتبہ ہو جاتی ہے اس لئے مدعی علیہ کا حاضر ہونا ضروری ہے ۔ اور جب مدعی علیہ کا حاضر ہونا ضروری ہے تو قضاء علی الغائب نا جائز ہے۔

امام ابو یوسف کی دلیل کا جواب دیتے ہوئے صاحب عنایہ اور علامہ بدرالدین عینی صاحب بنایہ نے فرمایا کی دلیل استصحاب کے ذریعہ کسی چیز کو رفع تو کیا جا سکتا ہے مگر ثابت نہیں کیا جا سکتا۔ اور یہاں حکم قضاء کو ثابت کرنے کی ضرورت ہے نہ کہ رفع کرنے کی ۔ پس اس جگہ استصحاب کے ذریعہ وقتِ قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار ثابت نہ ہو گا ۔ اور جب وقت قضاء تک انکار پر اصرار ثابت نہیں ہوا تو قضائے قاضی کی شرط بھی نہیں پائی گئی اور جب قضائے قاضی کی شرط نہیں پائی گئی تو مذکورہ صورت میں قاضی کا فیصلہ دینا بھی نا جائز ہوگا۔
In any case, the summary is that the rulings of judgment differ due to the admission of the defendant and the denial of the defendant. Therefore, in the absence of the defendant, the direction of the judge's judgment becomes ambiguous, so the presence of the defendant is necessary. And when the presence of the defendant is necessary, then judgment against the absent is impermissible. If the defendant denies the claim and then disappears before the judge's decision, the same ruling applies. And if he denies and then disappears, the answer is the same, because the condition is the existence of denial at the time of judgment, and in this there is a disagreement of Abu Yusuf. And whoever takes his place may be a deputy by his appointment, like a agent, or by deputyship of the law, like an executor appointed by the judge, and may be a constructive deputy, such that what is claimed against the absent is a cause for what he claims against the present. And this is in many forms in the books. But if it is a condition for his right, then it is not considered in making him a representative against the absent, and the full discussion is known in al-Jami'. Translation: If the defendant denies and then disappears, the same ruling applies. Because the existence of denial at the time of judgment is a condition, and in this there is the disagreement of Imam Abu Yusuf. And a person who is the representative of the defendant may sometimes be a deputy by his appointment, like an agent, or by deputyship of the law, like an executor from the judge, and sometimes a constructive deputy, in that what is claimed against the absent is a cause for what is claimed against the present. And this is mentioned in many forms in the books. In any case, if it is a condition for the proof of the plaintiff's right, then this condition is not considered in making the present a deputy on behalf of the absent, and all this discussion is in al-Jami' al-Saghir. Explanation: The author of al-Hidayah said that if the defendant denies the claim and disappears before the judge issues a decision, the same ruling applies: the judge should not decide, otherwise his decision will not be effective. Because the existence of denial at the time of judgment is a condition, and due to the absence of the defendant, denial is not found from the defendant at the time of judgment. So when the condition of judgment is not fulfilled, then the judgment of the judge is also not permissible in this case. But in this case, there is the disagreement of Imam Abu Yusuf, meaning according to him, in the mentioned case, the judge can decide. Because Imam Abu Yusuf says that for the judge's judgment, persistence on denial until the time of judgment is a condition. And when the defendant denies and then disappears and does not appear by the time of judgment, due to istishab (presumption of continuity), meaning keeping the previous state, it will be said that he remains and persists on his denial. So when the condition of the judge's decision, i.e., persistence on denial until the time of judgment, is found, then the judge's decision is also permissible. Responding to the argument of Imam Abu Yusuf, the author of al-'Inayah and Allamah Badr al-Din al-'Ayni, author of al-Binayah, said that by means of istishab, something can be removed but cannot be established. And here the need is to establish the ruling of judgment, not to remove it. Therefore, in this place, persistence on denial until the time of judgment cannot be established by istishab. And when persistence on denial until the time of judgment is not established, then the condition of the judge's judgment is also not fulfilled. And when the condition of the judge's judgment is not fulfilled, then in the mentioned case, the judge's decision is also impermissible.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins by stating the general rule: judgment against an absent defendant is not permissible because the presence of the defendant is necessary for the judgment to be valid. The defendant's presence allows him to either admit or deny the claim. If he is absent, the judge's direction becomes ambiguous. Then it addresses a specific case: if the defendant denies the claim and then disappears before the judge decides, the same rule applies—the judge cannot pass judgment. The reason is that the condition for valid judgment is the existence of denial at the time of judgment. Since the defendant is absent at that time, the condition is not met. However, Imam Abu Yusuf disagrees. He argues that persistence on denial until the time of judgment is the condition, and by istishab (presumption of continuity), we assume the defendant still persists on his denial because he denied before disappearing. Therefore, the condition is met and judgment is permissible. The passage then provides the refutation from later Hanafi scholars (Sahib al-'Inayah and al-'Ayni): istishab can remove a ruling but cannot establish one. Here, we need to establish the condition of persistence, which istishab cannot do. Hence, the ruling remains that judgment is impermissible. This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).

Key Terms
قضاء علی الغائب (qada' 'ala al-gha'ib): The act of a judge passing a ruling against a defendant who is not present in court.
انکار (inkar): The defendant's verbal denial of the plaintiff's claim.
استصحاب (istishab): A rational proof that presumes the continuation of a previous state until a change is proven.
شرط (shart): A condition that must be fulfilled for the legal act (judgment) to be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school, if a defendant denies a claim and then disappears before the judge issues a ruling, what is the ruling?
A) The judge can still pass a valid judgment.
B) The judgment is invalid because the condition of denial at the time of judgment is not met.
C) The judge must wait for the defendant to return.
D) The plaintiff's claim is automatically accepted.

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent defendant is invalid unless the condition of denial at the time of judgment is actually met; istishab cannot substitute for this condition.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Actual and Constructive Representatives for the Absent Defendant
Explains actual and constructive representatives for absent defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an actual representative (na'ib haqiqi) and a constructive representative (na'ib hukmi) in Hanafi judicial procedure, and explain when a present party can represent an absent defendant.

Core Idea
A representative for an absent defendant is either actual (appointed) or constructive (where the claim against the present is a necessary cause of the claim against the absent).

The Hanafi school generally prohibits judgment against an absent defendant, but allows it when a representative is present. Al-Quduri stated this rule, and Al-Hidayah elaborates on two types of representatives: actual (like an executor) and constructive (where the claim against the present is necessarily caused by the claim against the absent). This lesson unpacks the condition of 'necessary causation' and illustrates it with examples.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Recall that judgment against an absent defendant is impermissible unless there is a representative. This is the baseline from al-Quduri.
2
Identify the two types of representatives
Actual representative: appointed by the absent or by the judge (e.g., executor). Constructive representative: due to a legal connection between the claims.
3
Understand the condition for constructive representation
The claim against the absent must be the necessary cause (lazim sabab) of the claim against the present. Not occasional or conditional.
4
Apply to examples
In the first example (house usurpation and pre-emption), the claim against the absent seller is the cause of the claim against the present buyer. In the slave-girl example, the marriage is only an occasional cause, so no constructive representation.
5
Distinguish cause from condition
If the claim against the absent is merely a condition (shart) for the present's right, not a cause, constructive representation does not apply, as in the conditional divorce example.

Source Text

مکان میں شفعہ کا دعوی کیا ۔ پس عارف نے کہا کہ میں نے اس کو کسی سے نہیں خریدا بلکہ یہ میرا آبائی مکان ہے۔ پس واصف نے بینہ پیش کیا کہ عارف نے یہ مکان ساجد جو اس وقت غائب ہے اس سے خریدا ہے اور ساجد اس کو کیا ہے اور مدعی علی الغائب یعنی نکاح اس وقت تک تو باندی واپس کرنے کا سبب ہو گا جب تک نکاح باقی ہو۔ لیکن اگر شوہر خریدنے سے پہلے ہی طلاق دے چکا تو اب یہ عیب نکاح چونکہ زائل ہو گیا ہے اس لئے باندی واپس کرنے کا سبب نہ ہوگا۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ اس مسئلہ میں نکاح ایک وقت میں اگر باندی واپس کرنے کا سبب ہے تو دوسرے وقت میں واپس کرنے کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ لہذا اس مسئلہ میں کسی کو نائب حکمی قرار نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ اگر ایسا ہو کہ غائب پر جو کچھ دعوی ہے وہ حاضر پر ثبوت حق کی شرط ہو اور سبب نہ ہو تو غائب کی طرف سے حاضر کو نائب حکمی ٹھہرانے میں اس شرط کا اعتبار نہ ہو گا۔ یعنی اس صورت میں حاضر غائب کا نائب اور قائم مقام شمار نہ ہو گا۔ مثلاً ایک شخص نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ اگر خالد نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دی تو تو بھی طلاق والی ہے ۔ یعنی تجھ کو بھی طلاق ہے ۔ پھر قائل کی بیوی نے دعوی کیا کہ خالد نے اپنی بیوی کو طلاق دیدی ہے۔ اور اس پر بینہ پیش کر دیا تو اس کا یہ بینہ قبول نہ ہوگا ۔
Sahib al-Quduri stated that judgment against the absent is impermissible unless there is a representative of the absent defendant. So from 'whoever takes his place', the author of al-Hidayah is mentioning the details of the representative. Thus he said: The person who is the representative of the defendant may sometimes be truly his representative, like an executor appointed by the judge, and sometimes a constructive representative, in that what is claimed against the absent is necessarily the cause of what is claimed against the present. It should not be that sometimes it is a cause and sometimes not. The author of al-Hidayah said: Constructive representation is mentioned in many cases in the books. For example: Khalid claims against Hamid: 'This house, which Hamid is occupying, is my property. I bought it from Shahid at a time when Shahid was its owner, although Shahid is currently absent. And Hamid has seized this house from me, and Hamid denies this.' So Khalid the plaintiff presents evidence. Then Khalid's evidence will be accepted. And whatever ruling is given for Khalid will be effective against Hamid (the usurper) as well as against Shahid the absent, because the cause of the claim against Hamid is that he bought from Shahid, so Hamid becomes a constructive representative of Shahid. Thus the defendant (the seller) i.e., Shahid, although absent, has Hamid present as his constructive deputy. And another example: Wasif claims pre-emption against Arif regarding a house. Arif says: 'I did not buy it from anyone; this is my ancestral house.' Then Wasif presents evidence that Arif bought this house from Sajid, who is currently absent, and Sajid was the owner at the time of sale. And due to being a pre-emptor, I seek pre-emption. Then the ruling of buying will be given against Arif, and the ruling of selling against Sajid. And Arif will be considered the constructive deputy and representative of Sajid, because the cause of the claim of pre-emption against Arif is that Arif bought the house from Sajid. So in this issue, the defendant (the seller) i.e., Sajid, although absent, has his constructive deputy (Arif) present. And if the claim against the absent is not necessarily a cause for the claim against the present, but sometimes a cause and sometimes not, then this cause will not be considered, and he will not be counted as a constructive deputy. For example: A person buys a slave-girl. Then the buyer claims that the master of this slave-girl married her to a certain absent person, and he presents evidence. Then he wants to return her due to the defect of marriage. Then his evidence will not be accepted either in favor of the present (the master) nor in favor of the absent (the husband). Because the buyer claimed two things: one, a claim against the present (master) for returning the slave-girl due to defect, and second, a claim against the absent (husband) regarding the marriage. And the claim against the absent (i.e., the marriage) is only a cause for returning the slave-girl as long as the marriage remains. But if the husband had divorced her before the purchase, then this defect of marriage is removed, so it is not a cause for returning. So it is established that in this issue, the marriage is a cause for returning at one time but not at another. Therefore, in this issue, no one will be considered a constructive deputy. And if what is claimed against the absent is a condition for proving the right against the present and not a cause, then this condition is not considered in making the present a constructive deputy for the absent. That is, in this case, the present will not be counted as the deputy and representative of the absent. For example: A person says to his wife: 'If Khalid divorces his wife, then you are also divorced.' Then the speaker's wife claims that Khalid has divorced his wife, and she presents evidence. So her evidence will not be accepted.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage explains the types of representatives (qa'im maqam) that make judgment against an absent defendant permissible. It first states the general rule from al-Quduri: judgment against an absent defendant is impermissible unless there is a representative. Then it categorizes representatives into two types: (1) actual representative, like an executor appointed by the judge, and (2) constructive representative, where the claim against the absent is the necessary cause of the claim against the present. It gives two examples where constructive representation applies: in a case of usurpation where the buyer is present and the seller is absent, and in a case of pre-emption where the buyer is present and the seller is absent. Then it shows a case where constructive representation does not apply: when the claim against the absent is only sometimes a cause (e.g., the marriage of a slave-girl which may or may not be a defect depending on divorce). Finally, it mentions a case where the claim against the absent is a condition for the present's right, not a cause, so no constructive representation (e.g., a conditional divorce). This passage is crucial for understanding the limits of the exception to the rule against judgment in absentia.

Key Terms
قائم مقام (qa'im maqam): A person who stands in place of the absent defendant, either by appointment or by legal construction.
نائب حقیقی (na'ib haqiqi): A deputy appointed by the defendant himself or by the court, like a guardian or executor.
نائب حکمی (na'ib hukmi): A person who is considered a deputy due to the legal connection between the claim against him and the claim against the absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, when can judgment against an absent defendant be permissible?
A) Always, because the judge has authority.
B) Only when the absent defendant has an actual representative, like a lawyer.
C) When there is either an actual representative or a constructive representative where the claim against the present is necessarily caused by the claim against the absent.
D) Never, under any circumstances.

Key Takeaway
Judgment against an absent defendant is permissible only when the absent has a representative, either actual (appointed) or constructive (where the claim against the present is a necessary cause of the claim against the absent).

The Rule and Two Types of Representatives for Judgment Against the Absent
Definition and illustration of representatives for absent defendants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on judgment against the absent and distinguish between an actual and a legal representative.

Core Idea
Without a representative, judgment against the absent is invalid; the representative is either actual or legal.

Judging an absent party is inherently problematic because the defendant cannot defend themselves. Hanafi fiqh allows it only when someone present stands in their place. Al-Quduri states the baseline rule, and al-Hidayah expands it by defining two categories of representatives: actual (like guardians) and legal (based on a necessary causal link). This lesson unpacks the rule and the first example.

Guided Path
1
State the base rule
Identify al-Quduri's ruling: judgment against the absent is not permissible unless a representative (qā'im maqām) is present.
This is the starting point for all discussion.
2
Define actual representative
Recognize that an actual representative is someone appointed, like a guardian or executor, who can be sued on behalf of the absent.
This type is straightforward.
3
Define legal representative
Understand that a legal representative (nā'ib hukmī) arises when the claim against the present is necessarily caused by the claim against the absent.
The cause must be constant, not occasional.
4
Work through the first example
Analyze the case of Khalid suing Hamid for a house: Hamid is present but Shahid (seller) is absent. The claim against Hamid (usurpation) requires proving that Shahid sold to Khalid, so Hamid becomes Shahid's legal deputy.
The judgment against Shahid is valid because Hamid represents him.

Book of the Etiquette of the Judge. The author (al-Quduri) said: Judgment against the absent is not permissible unless there is a representative of the absent defendant. Then the author of al-Hidayah details the representative. He said: The person who is the representative of the defendant is sometimes actually his representative, like a guardian appointed by the judge, and sometimes legally (hukman) his representative. In the sense that what is claimed against the absent is necessarily the cause of what is claimed against the present. It should not be such that sometimes it is the cause and sometimes not. The author of al-Hidayah said: Legal representation is mentioned in many cases in the books. For example, Khalid claimed against Hamid: 'This house which Hamid occupies is my property; I bought it from Shahid when he was its owner, while Shahid is absent, and Hamid usurped it from me.' Hamid denies. Then Khalid presents evidence (bayyina). Khalid's evidence is accepted. The judgment that is given for Khalid is effective against Hamid (the usurper) and also against the absent Shahid, because the claim against Hamid is based on his purchase from Shahid, so Hamid becomes a legal representative of Shahid. So the defendant (seller) Shahid is absent, but Hamid is his legal deputy present. Another example: Wasif claimed shuf'ah against Arif regarding a house. Arif said: 'I did not buy it from anyone; it is my ancestral house.' Wasif presented evidence that Arif bought this house from Sajid (who is currently absent) and Sajid was the owner at the time of sale, and as a pre-emptor I claim shuf'ah. Then the ruling is that Arif is considered the buyer and Sajid the seller. Arif is legally the deputy and representative of Sajid, because the claim of shuf'ah against Arif is based on his purchase from Sajid. So in this case, the defendant (seller Sajid) is absent, but his legal deputy (Arif) is present. And if the claim against the absent is not necessarily a cause for the claim against the present, but sometimes is a cause and sometimes not, then this cause is not considered and he is not a legal deputy. For example, a person bought a slave girl. The buyer claimed that the master of this slave girl married her to a certain absent person, and he presented evidence. Then he wanted to return her due to the defect of marriage. His evidence is accepted neither against the present (master) nor against the absent (husband), because the buyer claimed two things: first, against the present master (return due to defect), second, against the absent husband (marriage). The claim against the absent (marriage) is a cause for returning the slave girl only as long as the marriage remains. But if the husband divorced her before the purchase, then this defect (marriage) is removed and is no longer a cause for return. So it is proven that in this case, the marriage is sometimes a cause and sometimes not. Therefore, no one is considered a legal deputy. If the claim against the absent is a condition for establishing a right against the present, not a cause, then this condition is not considered in making the present a legal deputy for the absent. For example, a man said to his wife: 'If Khalid divorces his wife, you are divorced.' Then the wife claimed that Khalid did divorce his wife, and she presented evidence. Her evidence is not accepted, and the judge will not rule that divorce has occurred, because the claim against the absent (Khalid) is a condition for establishing the right (divorce) against the present (the wife). It has been stated before that a condition is not considered when making the present a legal deputy for the absent. So in this case also the condition is not considered. Here an objection arises: If a man said to his wife: 'If such-and-such person enters this house, you are divorced.' Then the wife presented evidence that the person entered the house. This evidence is accepted, and the judge will rule that the divorce has occurred, even though this case resembles the previous one—the claim against the absent is a condition for establishing the right against the present. So the condition should not be considered. The answer is: In the case of conditional entry into a house, there is no nullification of the absent's right, so this does not fall under judgment against the absent. When it is not of the category of judgment against the absent, the condition is considered. In the previous case, there is nullification of the absent (Khalid's) right, so the condition is not considered.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on al-Quduri's ruling that judgment against an absent defendant is not permissible unless there is a representative (qā'im maqām). The commentator from al-Hidayah explains that the representative can be actual (ḥaqīqatan), like a court-appointed guardian, or legal (ḥukman), based on a necessary causal connection between the claim against the present and the claim against the absent. The key condition for legal representation is that the claim against the absent must be the necessary and constant cause (sabab lāzim) of the claim against the present; if it is only occasional or conditional, legal representation is not established.

Several examples illustrate this. In the first example, a buyer (Khalid) sues a usurper (Hamid) for a house, presenting evidence that he bought it from an absent seller (Shahid). The claim against Hamid (usurpation) is necessarily caused by the claim against Shahid (sale), so Hamid becomes Shahid's legal deputy, and judgment against Shahid is valid. Similarly, in a pre-emption (shuf'ah) claim, the present defendant (Arif) is the buyer from the absent seller (Sajid); the claim of pre-emption against Arif arises necessarily from the sale, so Arif is Sajid's legal deputy. 

Conversely, if the connection is not necessary, as in the case of a purchased slave girl whose marriage to an absent person is claimed as a defect, the marriage may or may not be a cause for return (e.g., if already divorced), so legal representation is not established. Also, if the claim against the absent is merely a condition (sharṭ) for the right against the present, not a cause, it is not recognized. For example, a conditional divorce: 'If Khalid divorces his wife, you are divorced.' Here, Khalid's divorce is a condition for the wife's divorce, not a cause, so the wife's evidence of Khalid's divorce is not accepted, as it would nullify Khalid's right. However, if the condition does not nullify the absent's right (e.g., entry into a house), it is considered, because such a judgment is not against the absent but against the present based on a condition.

Key Terms
قضاء علی الغائب (qadā' 'alā al-ghā'ib): Judgment against an absent person who is not present in the court session.
قائم مقام (qā'im maqām): Representative of the absent defendant, either actual (e.g., guardian) or legal (de facto due to necessary causal link).
نائب حکمی (nā'ib hukmī): Legal deputy; a present person who is deemed to represent the absent due to a non-occasional causal connection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when is judgment against an absent party valid?
A) When the absent party has been notified by mail.
B) When there is a representative (qā'im maqām) of the absent present in court.
C) When the case involves property only.
D) When the judge is certain of the claim.

Key Takeaway
A representative, whether actual or legal, is necessary for valid judgment against an absent party in Hanafi fiqh.

Legal Deputy (Nā'ib Hukmī): When a Necessary Cause Is Required
Illustration of necessary cause for legal representation via two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of necessary and constant cause (sabab lāzim) to determine whether a legal representative exists.

Core Idea
Legal representation exists only if the claim against the absent is a necessary and non-occasional cause of the claim against the present.

The previous lesson established that a legal representative can arise from a causal link. This lesson deepens the analysis by clarifying that the cause must be 'lāzim'—constant and inseparable. Two contrasting examples from al-Hidayah illustrate this: the shuf'ah case where the cause is constant, and the slave-girl case where the cause is occasional, thus not establishing representation.

Guided Path
1
Recall the condition for legal representation
The claim against the absent must be a necessary and constant cause (sabab lāzim) of the claim against the present. It cannot be occasional.
This is the core criterion.
2
Analyze the shuf'ah example
Wasif claims shuf'ah against Arif for a house. Arif denies buying it. Wasif proves Arif bought it from Sajid (absent). The claim of pre-emption necessarily arises from the sale, so Arif is Sajid's legal deputy.
The sale is a constant cause of the pre-emption claim.
3
Analyze the slave-girl example
Buyer of a slave girl claims she has a defect: marriage to an absent man. He wants to return her. The marriage is a cause for return only while it exists; if the husband divorced before purchase, it is not a cause. Therefore, it is occasional, not constant—no legal representation.
The occasional nature prevents legal deputyship.
4
Contrast the two examples
In shuf'ah, the sale necessarily gives rise to the pre-emption claim. In the slave-girl case, the marriage may or may not be a defect, so it is not a necessary cause.
The key difference is constancy vs. occasion.

حکمی (عارف حاضر ہے۔ اور اگر مدعی علی الغائب مدعی علی الحاضر کے لئے لازمی طور پر سبب نہ ہو بلکہ ایک وقت میں سبب ہو اور دوسرے وقت میں سبب نہ ہو تو یہ سبب معتبر نہ ہو گا ۔ اور حکما نا ئب شمار نہ ہو گا۔ زائل ہو گیا ہے اس لئے باندی واپس کرنے کا سبب نہ ہوگا۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ اس مسئلہ میں نکاح ایک وقت میں اگر باندی واپس کرنے کا سبب ہے تو دوسرے وقت میں واپس کرنے کا سبب نہیں ہے۔ لہذا اس مسئلہ میں کسی کو
