And it is permissible for him to hear the bayyinah (evidence) and to rule based on nukul (refusal to swear) and likewise based on iqrar (confession), because it is a ruling in accordance with Shari'ah. And if he informs about the iqrar of one of the two litigants or about the reliability of witnesses while both are still holding him as their arbitrator, his statement is accepted because his authority (wilayah) exists. And if he informs about the judgment (hukm), his statement is not accepted because his authority has ended, like the statement of the ruler after removal. Translation: It is permissible for the arbitrator to hear the bayyinah and to rule on the refusal to take an oath, and likewise to rule on confession, because he has made a ruling in accordance with Shari'ah. And if the arbitrator informed about the confession of one of the two parties or about the reliability of witnesses, while both are still holding him as arbitrator, his statement is accepted because his authority is established. And if he informed about the judgment, his statement is not accepted because his authority has ended, just as the statement of a qadi after removal is not accepted. Elaboration: Sahib Quduri said: When two people have appointed someone as their arbitrator, it is permissible for the arbitrator that if the plaintiff presents bayyinah, he hears it. And if the plaintiff has no bayyinah, he administers an oath to the defendant, and if the defendant denies the right, the arbitrator gives judgment in favor of the plaintiff. Similarly, if the defendant confesses to the plaintiff's right, the arbitrator gives judgment on that confession. The proof is that hearing bayyinah, giving judgment on the defendant's refusal to swear, and giving judgment on confession are all things in accordance with Shari'ah, and the arbitrator is authorized to do every act in accordance with Shari'ah, so he is authorized to do these things. And if the arbitrator says to one of the plaintiff or defendant: 'You confessed before me to your companion's right and I gave judgment on that confession, or evidence was presented before me against you and I verified the witnesses, so I gave judgment against you based on the evidence,' then this statement of the arbitrator is accepted provided that both persons are still holding him as arbitrator, because his authority remains due to his being arbitrator. So if the person against whom judgment was given denies having confessed before the arbitrator or having had evidence established against him, the statement of that defendant is not considered; rather, the qadi accepts the arbitrator's statement and implements his judgment, because as long as the arbitrator is an arbitrator, he is authorized to judge like a qadi. And if the arbitrator informs that 'I have already given judgment against you,' his statement 'I have given judgment' is not accepted because when the arbitrator gives judgment, he becomes removed from office, and when removed, his authority is cut off, so his statement 'I gave such-and-such judgment' is not accepted, just as after removal, the qadi's statement 'I gave judgment' is not accepted.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage details the procedural powers of a hakam (arbitrator) during the arbitration process. The text begins by stating that a hakam is permitted to hear evidence (bayyinah), to give judgment based on the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul), and to accept a confession (iqrar). The reason is that these actions are all in accordance with Shari'ah, and the hakam is authorized to do whatever is Shari'ah-compliant. The commentary by Sahib Quduri (Imam al-Quduri) elaborates: when two parties appoint a hakam, if the plaintiff brings evidence, the hakam hears it; if not, he asks the defendant for an oath; if the defendant refuses to swear, the hakam gives judgment for the plaintiff; if the defendant confesses, the hakam gives judgment on that confession. The proof is that all three are in line with Shari'ah, and the hakam is authorized to do whatever is in accordance with Shari'ah. The text then addresses a critical procedural point: if the hakam informs about something that happened during the arbitration, such as a confession or witness verification (tazkiyah), while the parties still consider him their hakam, his statement is accepted because his authority (wilayah) is ongoing. However, if he informs about the final judgment itself, his statement is not accepted, because once he gives judgment, his authority ends; he becomes like a qadi who has been removed from office. The 'at-tashrih' commentary explains that if the hakam says 'you confessed and I gave judgment,' the qadi accepts the hakam's word over the denial of the party against whom judgment was given, as long as the hakam is still in office. But if the hakam says 'I gave judgment,' after having given it, his word is not accepted. This distinction is crucial: the hakam's report about a fact (iqrar or tazkiyah) is trusted because his authority continues; but his report about the judgment itself is not trusted because his authority ended with the judgment.

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence, usually testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, presented in court.
نکول (nukūl): Refusal to take an oath when required; in Hanafi law, if the defendant refuses to swear after the plaintiff has no evidence, the qadi gives judgment for the plaintiff.
اقرار (iqrār): Confession of a right against oneself; in judicial context, it is conclusive evidence.
ولایت (wilāyah): Authority, jurisdiction; the hakam's power to act as an arbitrator while in office.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a hakam gives a judgment and then informs the qadi that he has done so, what is the ruling on accepting his statement?

Key Takeaway
A hakam may hear evidence, rule on nukul, and accept iqrar, but his report of the judgment after its delivery is not accepted because his wilayah ends with the judgment.

Rulings on Hakam's Judgment for Relatives and Joint Sitting of Arbitrators
Two rulings on qada and arbitration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two rulings concerning a hakam's judgment for relatives and the requirement for multiple arbitrators to sit together, and explain the rationale of tuhmah.

Core Idea
A judge's judgment for close relatives is invalid due to tuhmah; multiple arbitrators must act jointly.

This passage from Al-Hidayah presents two key principles of Islamic adjudication. First, a judge or arbitrator may not pass a judgment in favor of his close relatives because the suspicion of bias (tuhmah) invalidates it, just as it would reject his testimony. Second, if two people are appointed as arbitrators, they must sit together and decide collectively, because arbitration requires deliberation. These rules ensure procedural integrity and justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and locate the two distinct legal rulings: one about judgment for/against relatives, and one about multiple arbitrators.
The first ruling uses analogy from testimony; the second is a direct condition.
2
Understand the qiyas from testimony
The invalid judgment for relatives is based on the same logic as rejected testimony. Recognize that the operative cause (illah) is tuhmah (suspicion of bias).
If testimony for relatives is rejected due to tuhmah, the same applies to judgment.
3
Contrast judgment for vs. against relatives
Note that the ruling is specific: judgment in favor (valid) is invalid; judgment against them is valid because tuhmah does not apply.
Tuhmah only arises when the judge benefits the relative.
4
Establish the rule for multiple arbitrators
When two arbitrators are appointed, they must meet together. Deliberation requires collective opinion, not separate decisions.
This prevents contradictory rulings and ensures consultation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم الحاكم لا بويه و زوجته و ولده باطل والمولى وللمحكم فيه سواء وهذا لانه لا تقبل شهادته لهؤلاء لمكان التهمة فكذلك لا يصح القضاء لهم بخلاف ما اذا حكم عليهم لانه تقبل شهادته عليهم لانتفاء التهمة فكذا القضاء ولو حكـمـا رجلين لا بد من اجتماعهما لانه امر يحتاج فيه الى الرأى والله اعلم بالصواب ترجمہ اور حاکم کا حکم اپنے والدین، بیوی ، اولاد کے لئے باطل ہے اور اس میں قاضی اور حکم دونوں برابر ہیں۔ اور یہ اس لئے کہ ان لوگوں کے لئے حاکم کی گواہی تہمت کی وجہ سے قبول نہیں ہے۔ یا اسی طرح ان لوگوں کے واسطے حکم قضاء بھی صحیح نہ ہو گا بر خلاف اس کے جب ان لوگوں کے خلاف حکم دیا ہو ۔ کیونکہ ان لوگوں کے خلاف اس کی گواہی تہمت کے منتفی ہونے کی وجہ سے قبول کی جاتی ہے۔ پس اسی طرح حکم قضاء بھی ۔ اور اگر دو شخصوں نے دو آدمیوں کو حکم بنایا تو ان دونوں کا جمع ہونا ضروری ہے اس لئے کہ حکم دینا ایسا امر ہے جس
And the judgment of the hakim (ruler/judge) for his parents, his wife, and his child is invalid, and the same applies to the mahkum (the one who is judged?) and the arbitrator; they are equal in this. This is because his testimony for these people is not accepted due to suspicion (tuhmah), so likewise his judgment (qada) for them is not valid. Contrary to when he gives judgment against them, because his testimony against them is accepted due to the absence of suspicion, so similarly his judgment. And if two people appoint two men [as arbitrators], it is necessary for them to assemble together, because it is a matter that requires opinion. And Allah knows best what is correct. Translation: And the judgment of the ruler for his parents, wife, and children is invalid, and the same applies to the qadi and the arbitrator; they are equal in this. This is because his testimony for these people is not accepted due to suspicion, so likewise his judgment (qada) for them is not valid. Contrary to when he gives judgment against them, because his testimony against them is accepted due to the absence of suspicion, so similarly his judgment. And if two people appointed two men [as arbitrators], it is necessary for them to assemble together, because judgment is a matter that requires opinion.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rulings. First, a judge (qadi) or arbitrator (hakam) may not render a judgment in favor of his own close relatives (parents, wife, children) because such a judgment would be invalid. The reasoning is analogical to the rule on testimony: a qadi's testimony for these relatives is not accepted due to suspicion of bias (tuhmah). Similarly, his judgment for them is invalid. However, if he judges against these relatives, the judgment is valid because his testimony against them is accepted due to lack of suspicion. The rule applies equally to a hakam (arbitrator) as to a qadi. Second, if two people appoint two arbitrators (instead of one), those two must sit together and make their decision jointly. This is because arbitration is a matter that requires deliberation and opinion (ra'y). The text implies that a single arbitrator may act alone, but multiple arbitrators must act collectively.

Key Terms
تہمت (tuhmah): Suspicion of partiality or bias, which prevents acceptance of testimony or judgment in favor of relatives.
حاکم (ḥākim): Here synonymous with qadi (judge) or hakam (arbitrator), but in context of judgment.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Judgment, adjudication; the formal decision issued by a qadi or hakam.
اجتماع (ijtimā‘): Assembly, sitting together; required for multiple arbitrators to deliberate jointly.

B) They must be appointed by a qadi

D) They must each give a separate opinion

Key Takeaway
A judge's judgment for close relatives is invalid due to tuhmah, and multiple arbitrators must deliberate jointly.

When an Arbitrator's Word is Binding
Explains when an arbitrator's statement is accepted and when not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on accepting an arbitrator's statement and distinguish between statements made during his tenure and after verdict.

Core Idea
An arbitrator's statement is accepted while his authority lasts; after verdict, his word is unenforceable.

This lesson from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains a subtle but crucial point in Hanafi judicial procedure: when can an arbitrator's report of his own actions be trusted? The answer hinges on whether his authority (wilayah) is still active or whether it has terminated with the issuance of a verdict.

Guided Path
1
State the two-part ruling
The passage divides the arbitrator's statements into two categories: (1) statements about actions performed while still in office (e.g., hearing confession or evidence) are accepted; (2) statements about a final verdict already given are not accepted.
The key criterion is the continuity of wilayah.
2
Identify the legal basis (dalil)
The proof is that the arbitrator is authorized to perform acts in accordance with Shari'ah (hearing evidence, administering oaths, judging on confession). However, the distinction comes from the analogy to a qadi after dismissal: just as a dismissed qadi's statement about a past verdict is not accepted, so too an arbitrator's statement after his task ends.
The dalil here is not a single text but a combination of general authorization and qiyas.
3
Apply the condition of wilayah
The acceptance of the statement depends on the arbitrator's authority being intact. If he has issued a final verdict, his role is complete and his authority ends. Therefore, his mere claim of having judged cannot revive his authority.
This is a Hanafi position emphasizing the termination of jurisdiction upon completion of the task.
4
Understand the practical consequence
If the arbitrator says he already judged, the losing party can deny it and the qadi will not enforce based on the arbitrator's word alone. This protects against false claims and maintains procedural integrity.
The thamarah al-khilaf is that the case may need to be reheard if the arbitrator's statement is not accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تو حکم کا قول قبول کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ ولایت قائم ہے۔ اور اگر حکم نے حکم کی خبر دی تو اس کا قول قبول نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس کی ولایت ختم ہوگئی جیسے معزول ہونے کے بعد قاضی کا قول قبول نہیں ہوگا۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے فرمایا کہ جب دو شخصوں نے کسی کو اپنا حکم مقرر کیا تو حکم کے لئے یہ بات جائز ہے کہ مدعی اگر بینہ پیش کرے تو اس کے بینہ کی سماعت کرے۔ اور اگر مدعی کے پاس پینہ نہ ہونے کی صورت میں مدعی علیہ سے قسم دلائے اور مدعی علیہ حق کا انکار کر دے تو حکم مدعی کے حق میں فیصلہ کر دے اسی طرح اگر مدعی علیہ نے مدعی کے حق کا اقرار کر لیا تو اس اقرار پر بھی حکم فیصلہ دیدے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ بینہ کی سماعت، مدعی علیہ کے انکار قسم پر فیصلہ دینا اور اقرار پر حکم دینا تینوں باتیں شریعت کے موافق ہیں اور حکم شریعت کے موافق ہر کام کرنے کا مجاز ہوتا ہے لہذا ان باتوں کے کرنے کا بھی مجاز ہوگا۔
اور اگر حکم نے مدعی یا مدعی علیہ میں سے کسی ایک سے یہ کہا تو نے میرے پاس اپنے ساتھی کے حق کا اقرار کیا ہے اور اس اقرار کی بنیاد پر فیصلہ دے دیا ، یا تیرے خلاف میرے سامنے سینہ پیش کر دیا گیا اور ان گواہوں کی تعدیل بھی کر دی گئی ہے۔ پس بینہ کی وجہ سے میں نے تیرے خلاف حکم دیدیا ہے تو حکم کا یہ قول قبول ہوگا بشرطیکہ دونوں شخص اس کے حکم بنانے پر قائم ہوں۔ کیونکہ حکم ہونے کی وجہ سے حکم کی ولایت باقی ہے چنانچہ جس کے خلاف حکم نے فیصلہ دیا ہے اگر اس نے حکم کے پاس اقرار کرنے کا انکار کر دے، یا اپنے خلاف ہینہ قائم ہونے کا انکار کر دے تو اس مقضیٰ علیہ کے قول کی طرف التفات نہیں کیا جائے گا، بلکہ قاضی حکم کا قول قبول کر کے اس کا فیصلہ نافذ کرے گا کیونکہ حکم جب تک حکم ہے قاضی کی طرح حکم کرنے کا مجاز ہے۔ اور اگر حکم نے یہ خبر دی کہ میں تیرے خلاف فیصلہ کر چکا ہوں۔ تو اس کا یہ قول کہ میں فیصلہ کر چکا ہوں قبول نہ ہوگا کیونکہ حکم جب فیصلہ کر چکا تو وہ معزول ہو گیا اور جب معزول ہو گیا تو اس کی ولایت بھی منقطع ہو گئی تو اس کا قول انی حکمت بكذا ( میں اس کا حکم کر چکا ہوں ) بھی قبول نہ ہوگا۔ جیسے معزول ہونے کے بعد قاضی کا قول انسی حکمت ( میں حکم کر چکا ) قبول نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔
So his statement will be accepted because his authority (wilayah) is established. But if the arbitrator (hakam) informs about the verdict, then his statement will not be accepted because his authority has ended, just as the statement of a judge after dismissal is not accepted.
Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said: When two people appoint someone as their arbitrator, it is permissible for the arbitrator to hear the evidence (bayyinah) if the plaintiff presents it, and if the plaintiff has no evidence, he may administer an oath to the defendant, and if the defendant denies the right, the arbitrator may give a verdict in favor of the plaintiff. Similarly, if the defendant confesses to the plaintiff's right, the arbitrator may also give a verdict based on that confession. The proof is that hearing evidence, giving a verdict on the defendant's denial and oath, and judging based on confession—these three things are in accordance with the Shari'ah, and the arbitrator is authorized to do everything in accordance with the Shari'ah, so he is also authorized to do these things.
And if the arbitrator says to either the plaintiff or the defendant: 'You confessed in my presence regarding the right of your companion, and I gave a verdict on the basis of that confession,' or 'Evidence was presented before me against you, and the witnesses were also verified (ta'dil), so on the basis of that evidence I gave a verdict against you,' then this statement of the arbitrator will be accepted, provided that the two persons remain as his appointer. Because due to being an arbitrator, his authority continues. So if the person against whom the verdict was given denies having confessed before the arbitrator, or denies the establishment of evidence against him, no attention will be paid to the statement of this person against whom the verdict was given (maqdi 'alayhi). Rather, the judge (qadi) will accept the arbitrator's statement and enforce his verdict, because as long as the arbitrator is an arbitrator, he is authorized to judge like a judge. And if the arbitrator informs that 'I have already given a verdict against you,' then his statement 'I have given a verdict' will not be accepted, because when the arbitrator has given the verdict, he becomes dismissed (ma'zul), and when he becomes dismissed, his authority is severed, so his statement 'innī ḥakamtu bi-kadhā' (I judged such) will also not be accepted, just as after dismissal, the judge's statement 'innī ḥakamtu' (I judged) is not accepted.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof (dalil) from the Hanafi school regarding the acceptance of an arbitrator's statement about his past actions. The passage is from Ashraf al-Hidayah, a commentary on al-Hidayah. The core ruling distinguishes between two scenarios:
1. If the arbitrator informs that he has heard a confession or received evidence (i.e., he is still in the process of adjudicating), his statement is accepted because his authority (wilayah) as arbitrator is ongoing. The parties are still under his jurisdiction, and he has the power to decide. Therefore, if someone denies the confession or evidence, the qadi (judge) will rely on the arbitrator's word and enforce his decision.
2. If the arbitrator informs that he has already given a final verdict (i.e., he has completed his task), his statement is NOT accepted. This is because once the arbitrator delivers a verdict, his authority ends—he is effectively dismissed (ma'zul). Just as a qadi's statement about a past verdict is not accepted after his dismissal, the same applies to an arbitrator. The reasoning is that after the verdict, the arbitrator no longer has any jurisdiction, so his uncorroborated claim cannot be relied upon.
The proof (dalil) provided is that hearing evidence, administering oaths, and judging on confessions are all permissible acts in Shari'ah, and the arbitrator is authorized to perform them. The acceptance of his statement depends on the continuity of his authority.

Key Terms
حكم (ḥakam): An arbitrator appointed by disputing parties to resolve their conflict; has limited authority compared to a qadi.
ولایت (wilāyah): Authority or jurisdiction; here, the hakam's legal capacity to adjudicate while his appointment stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) No, unless he produces witnesses.

Key Takeaway
An arbitrator's statement is only accepted as evidence if he is still in office; after verdict, his authority ceases, and his word alone is not binding.

Invalidity of Judge's Ruling for Relatives
States and explains why a judge/arbitrator's ruling in favor of close relatives is invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds a judge's ruling in favor of close relatives invalid and apply the analogy from testimony.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling for relatives is invalid because his testimony for them is not accepted.

The Hanafi school links the capacity to judge with the capacity to testify. This lesson explores the ruling that a qadi or arbitrator cannot give a valid judgment in favor of his parents, wife, or children, based on the analogy from shahadah. The key concept is tuhma—accusation of bias. Understanding this ruling helps grasp how procedural integrity is maintained through preventive measures in Islamic jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Determine what exactly is being ruled: the judgment of a qadi or hakam in favor of his parents, wife, or children is batil. Note that this applies equally to a qadi and a muhakkam.
2
Trace the proof: analogy from testimony
The proof is qiyas from shahadah. Since the judge's testimony is not accepted for these relatives due to tuhma, his judicial ruling in their favor is also invalid.
The condition for being a judge is ahliyyat al-shahada.
3
Examine the 'illa (effective cause)'
The common cause is tuhma (accusation of bias). This exists both in testimony and judgment for relatives, so the ruling transfers.
Tuhma is a preventive cause (mani') that nullifies validity.
4
Distinguish rulings in favor vs. against
If the judge rules against relatives, the ruling is valid because tuhma does not exist in that direction. Testimony against relatives is accepted, so judgment is also valid.
This is the exception mentioned in the passage.
5
Consider cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow a judge's ruling for relatives if no actual bias is proven. In the Hanafi school, the mere possibility invalidates it.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكم الحاكم لا بويه و زوجته و ولده باطل والمولى وللمحكم فيه سواء وهذا لانه لا تقبل شهادته لهؤلاء لمكان التهمة فكذلك لا يصح القضاء لهم بخلاف ما اذا حكم عليهم لانه تقبل شهادة عليهم لانتفاء التهمة فكذا القضاء
ترجمہ اور حاکم کا حکم اپنے والدین، بیوی ، اولاد کے لئے باطل ہے اور اس میں قاضی اور حکم دونوں برابر ہیں۔ اور یہ اس لئے کہ ان لوگوں کے لئے حاکم کی گواہی تہمت کی وجہ سے قبول نہیں ہے۔ یا اسی طرح ان لوگوں کے واسطے حاکم کا حکم قضاء بھی صحیح نہ ہو گا بر خلاف اس کے جب ان لوگوں کے خلاف حکم دیا ہو ۔ کیونکہ ان لوگوں کے خلاف اس کی گواہی تہمت کے منتفی ہونے کی وجہ سے قبول کی جاتی ہے۔ پس اسی طرح حکم قضاء بھی ۔
تشریح صاحب قدوری نے کہا کہ حاکم ، قاضی ہو یا حکم ہوا اگر اس نے اپنے والدین یا اولا دیا بیوی کے موافق کوئی حکم دیا تو وہ حکم معتبر نہ ہوگا بلکہ باطل ہوگا۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ قضاء کے لئے اہلیت شہادت شرط ہے یعنی جو شخص شہادت کا اہل ہوگا اور وہی قاضی اور حکم بنے کا اہل ہوگا۔ اور چونکہ اندیشہ تہمت کی وجہ سے کسی کی شہادت اس کے والدین، اولاد، اور بیوی کے حق میں مقبول نہیں ہے اس لئے اُن کے حق میں اس کا حکم اور فیصلہ بھی مقبول نہ ہوگا۔ ہاں اگر کسی شخص نے بحیثیت قاضی یا بحیثیت حکم اپنے والدین یا اولا دیا بیوی کے خلاف حکم دیا تو اس کو قبول کر لیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں امکان تہمت نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے ان کے خلاف گواہی قبول کر لی جاتی ہے لہذا ان کے خلاف فیصلہ اور حکم بھی قبول کر لیا جائے گا۔
And the ruling of the ruler (hakim) for his parents, wife, and child is invalid, and [this applies] equally to the qadi and the arbitrator (muhakkam). This is because his testimony for these people is not accepted due to the presence of accusation (tuhma), so similarly, ruling (qada) in their favor is not valid. Contrary to when he rules against them, because his testimony against them is accepted due to the absence of accusation, so likewise [his] ruling [is valid].
Translation: And the ruling of the ruler for his parents, wife, and children is invalid, and in this the qadi and the arbitrator are equal. And this is because the ruler's testimony for these people is not accepted due to accusation. So similarly, the ruler's judgment for these people will not be valid. Contrary to when he has ruled against these people, because his testimony against them is accepted due to the absence of accusation. So likewise [his] judgment [is valid].
Explanation: Sahib al-Quduri said: Whether the ruler is a qadi or an arbitrator, if he gives a ruling in favor of his parents, children, or wife, that ruling is not valid (mu'tabar) but is invalid (batil).
The proof is that for qada (judgment), eligibility for testimony (ahliyyat al-shahada) is a condition. That is, a person who is eligible for testimony is the one who is eligible to be a qadi or hakam. And since, due to the fear of accusation, a person's testimony in favor of his parents, children, and wife is not accepted, therefore his ruling and judgment in their favor will also not be accepted. However, if a person, whether as a qadi or as an arbitrator, gives a ruling against his parents, children, or wife, then it will be accepted. Because in that case, due to the absence of possibility of accusation, testimony against them is accepted, so judgment and ruling against them will also be accepted.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This is a ruling with a clear analogy (qiyas) in the Hanafi school, as presented in al-Hidayah. The passage establishes that it is prohibited (invalid) for a judge (qadi) or an arbitrator (hakam) to give a ruling in favor of his own parents, spouse, or children. The reason given is 'tuhma'—the accusation of bias or partiality. The proof is based on an analogy from testimony (shahadah): since a judge's testimony is not accepted in favor of these relatives due to potential bias, his judicial ruling is also invalid for them. This is because the condition for being a judge is that one must be eligible to testify (ahliyyat al-shahada). The ruling also distinguishes between rulings in favor versus rulings against relatives: if the judge rules against them, the ruling is valid because there is no accusation of bias in that direction. In fact, the judge might be stricter. This is consistent with the principle that the same person's testimony against relatives is accepted. The passage emphasizes that this rule applies equally to an appointed qadi and a privately chosen arbitrator (muhakkam).

Key Terms
تہمت (tuhma): Accusation of bias or partiality that legally disqualifies a person from being a witness or judge in cases involving close relatives.
اہلیت شہادت (ahliyyat al-shahada): The legal capacity to be a witness; here, it is treated as a prerequisite for being a judge or arbitrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a qadi gives a verdict in favor of his wife?
A) Valid, because he is legally appointed.
B) Valid only if the wife is not a party to the case.
C) Invalid (batil), because his testimony would not be accepted for her.
D) Invalid only if there is proven bias.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling in favor of his immediate relatives is automatically invalid due to the principle of tuhma, upheld by analogy from testimony, and the same rule applies to arbitrators.

Qada — Judiciary
beginner

Two Arbitrators Must Agree on the Verdict
A ruling stating the necessity of joint agreement for two arbitrators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that two arbitrators must jointly agree on a verdict and explain the rationale based on the nature of arbitration as a matter of opinion (ra'y).

Core Idea
Two arbitrators must both agree; a single verdict is invalid.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Hanafi arbitration: when two parties appoint two arbitrators, the verdict requires the concurrence of both. The source passage from al-Hidayah explains that arbitration is a matter requiring ijtihad (opinion), and the appointment of two signals the parties' desire for a collective decision. This short but significant rule protects the integrity of arbitration by ensuring that both appointed decision-makers contribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Two disputing parties appoint two arbitrators to resolve their conflict.
2
State the ruling
The verdict must be agreed upon by both arbitrators; it is wajib.
3
Grasp the rationale
Arbitration requires ra'y (opinion) and ijtihad; the parties' appointment of two indicates they want joint deliberation.
4
Apply the consequence
A verdict given by only one arbitrator is invalid and unacceptable.
Even if the other is absent or refuses.
5
Note the Hanafi position
This is the mufta bihi view; some other schools may allow one to decide if the other is unavailable.
Cross-madhhab ikhtilaf exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو حكما رجلين لا بد من اجتماعهما لانه امر يحتاج فيه الى الرأى
ترجمہ اور اگر دو شخصوں نے دو آدمیوں کو حکم بنایا تو ان دونوں کا جمع ہونا ضروری ہے اس لئے کہ حکم دینا ایسا امر ہے جس میں اجتہاد رائے کی ضرورت ہے۔
تشریح اور اگر دو شخصوں نے دو آدمیوں کو اپنے نزاعی معاملہ میں حکم مقرر کیا تو فیصلہ پر دونوں کا اتفاق ضروری ہے کیونکہ بعض معاملات رائے اور مشورے کے محتاج ہوتے ہیں۔ پس مدعی اور مدعی علیہ دونوں شخصوں کا دو آدمیوں کو حکم مقرر کرنا اس بات کی علامت ہے کہ دونوں شخص ، دونوں حکموں کی رائے سے فیصلہ چاہتے ہیں اور جب مدعی اور مدعی علیہ دونوں اجتماعی طور پر دونوں حکموں کی رائے سے فیصلہ چاہتے ہیں تو ان دونوں میں سے اگر ایک حکم نے حکم دیا اور دوسرے نے حکم نہیں دیا تو یہ کم جائز اور قابل قبول نہ ہوگا ۔ واللہ اعلم بالصواب۔
And if two persons appoint two men [as arbitrators], it is necessary that they both gather (come to a joint decision), because it is an affair that requires opinion (ra'y).
Translation: And if two persons appoint two men as arbitrators, then it is obligatory for both of them to gather, because giving a verdict is such an affair that requires exertion of opinion (ijtihad al-ra'y).
Explanation: And if two persons appoint two men as arbitrators for their disputed matter, then agreement on the verdict is necessary for both, because some matters are in need of opinion and consultation. So the appointment of two men as arbitrators by both the plaintiff and the defendant is a sign that both persons want a verdict from the opinion of both arbitrators. And when both the plaintiff and the defendant collectively desire a verdict from the opinion of both arbitrators, then if one of them gives a verdict and the other does not, this decision will not be permissible and not acceptable. And Allah knows best the right.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling in the Hanafi school regarding the appointment of multiple arbitrators. The passage states that if two disputing parties each appoint an arbitrator (or jointly appoint two), the two arbitrators must agree on the verdict. The reason given is that adjudication (hukm) is a matter requiring opinion (ra'y) and ijtihad, and the parties' act of appointing two indicates that they want a decision based on collective deliberation. If only one arbitrator gives a verdict and the other disagrees, that verdict is not valid or acceptable. This ruling is based on the understanding that the authority of each arbitrator is conditional on their joint decision; neither can act alone. The commentary explains that the appointment of two is a sign that the parties seek the combined opinion of both. Therefore, a verdict from only one is not binding.

Key Terms
اجتماع (ijtimā‘): The agreement or joint decision of two arbitrators; literally 'gathering' but here meaning concurrence.
رائے (ra'y): Personal opinion or ijtihad; the arbitrators must exercise their judgment and reach a consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if two persons appoint two arbitrators, what is required for the verdict to be valid?
A) Only the arbitrator appointed by the plaintiff must decide.
B) The arbitrators must agree between themselves, even if one is absent.
C) Both arbitrators must agree on the verdict together.
D) The verdict of the more learned arbitrator is sufficient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi arbitration, two arbitrators must jointly agree on the verdict; a single arbitrator's decision is invalid.
